PREFACE
This thesis was completed at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU), Department
of Health Technology, in fulﬁlment of the requirements for acquiring the Master of Sci-
ence degree in the ﬁeld of biomedical engineering. The work presented in this thesis,
was the result of the collaboration between the Biomedical Signal Processing Research
Group at DTU, and the Danish Center for Sleep Medicine at Rigshospitalet Glostrup.
The project was accomplished between the 13th of July and the 20th of December 2020,
and corresponded to a workload of 30 ECTS points. The thesis presents the work done
in investigating speciﬁc characteristics throughout the sleep for patients with insomnia.
Enjoy!
Kongens Lyngby, December 2020

==PAGE:9==
ABSTRACT
Project description: Adequate sleep is central for health and normal cognitive func-
tioning. Poor sleep quality causes societal burden: insomnia, which is the most common
sleep disorder. However, the basic understanding of the sleep-wake processor is inad-
equate; an unmet need is the understanding of the relation of presence of macro-sleep
despite the perception of poor, light, or inadequate sleep. Therefore, the project aims to
use an automatically annotated dataset for analysis of arousal morphology, in order to
discover physiological features that are speciﬁc for insomnia patients.
Methodology: All three datasets had arousals automatically detected by a validated
detector, and were preprocessed afterwards. The detected arousals were investigated
through different features, e.g. amplitude, duration, and frequency. These were calcu-
lated throughout the entire sleep and in each sleep stage. The arousals were also related
to apnea events, to see if the arousals got affected by apneas, and if affected differently
in insomniacs and controls. Conventional sleep parameters, such as sleep latency, wake
after sleep onset etc., and features regarding the sleep stage transitions, were also tested
for signiﬁcant group differences.
Results: Only a few signiﬁcant group differences were found, and non were consis-
tently within all three datasets. The signiﬁcant group differences included: total sleep
time, wake after sleep onset, sleep efﬁciency, arousal duration in wake, variance of
arousal power in N1, and sleep stage transitions between N2 and wake, wake and REM,
as well as N3 and N2.
The apnea induced arousal features resulted in signiﬁcant group differences, throughout
all sleep stages in two datasets, with a few exceptions. The last dataset had only signiﬁ-
cant group differences in approximately half of the cases.
Conclusion: The typical ﬁndings and theory regarding insomnia are challenged by
the ﬁndings of this thesis, since no consistent group difference was found. However,
researching the apnea induced arousals might provide useful information, for deeper
understanding of the insomnia characteristics and classiﬁcation.
v
==PAGE:10==
==PAGE:11==
RESUMÉ
Projekt beskrivelse: Tilstrækkelig søvn er centralt for velfærd og normal kognitiv
funktion. En dårlig søvn kvalitet forårsager en samfundsmæssig byrde: insomni (søvn-
besvær), hvilket er den mest almindelige søvn sygdom. Imidlertidig er den grundlæggende
forståelse af søvn-vågen processen utilstrækkelig; et udækket behov er forståelsen af
forholdet mellem makro-søvn til trods for opfattelsen af dårlig, let, eller utilstrækkelig
søvn. Derfor er projektets hovedmål at bruge nogle automatisk detekterede datasæt, til
at analysere morphologien af disse opvågninger, for at opdage fysiologiske træk der er
speciﬁkke for insomni patienter.
Metode: Alle tre datasæt ﬁk automatisk detekteret opvågninger af en valideret detektor,
og blev efterfølgende behandlet. De detekterede opvågninger blev undersøgt gennem
forskellige træk, såsom amplitude, varighed, og frekvens. Disse blev udregnet gennem
hele søvnen og i hver søvnfase. Opvågningerne blev også relateret til apnø events, for at
undersøge om de blev påvirket af apnø, og om de blev påvirket forskelligt for insomni
patienter og kontroller. Konventionelle søvn parametre, såsom søvn reaktionstid (tiden
det tager at gå fra vågen til sovende), vågen efter søvn start osv., samt parametre ve-
drørende søvnstadie overgange blev også testet for signiﬁkante gruppeforskelle.
Resultater: Kun nogle få signiﬁkante forskelle blev fundet, og ingen var konsekvente
for alle tre datasæt. De signiﬁkante gruppe forskelle var: total søvn tid, vågen efter søvn
start, søvn effektiviteten, opvågnings varighed i vågen tilstand, variansen af opvågnings
styrken i søvnstadie 1, og overgange mellem søvnstadie 1 og vågen, mellem vågen og
dyb søvn, samt mellem søvnstadie 3 og 2.
De apnø inducerede opvågninger resulterede i signiﬁkante gruppe forskelle, i alle søvn-
stadier og i to datasæt med enkelte undtagelser. Det sidste datasæt havde kun sig-
niﬁkante gruppe forskelle i cirka halvdelen af tilfældende..
Konklusion: De typiske fund og teori vedrørende insomni udfordres af resultaterne i
denne afhandling, da ingen konsistente gruppe forskelle blev fundet. Yderligere under-
søgelse af de apnø inducerede opvågninger kan give nyttig information, til en dybere
forståelse af insomni karakteristikken og klassiﬁcering.

2.4
The "key switch" of the sleep-wake regulation. During sleep, VLPO
neurons are active, and hence a loss of VLPO neurons results in frag-
mented sleep and insomnia. Two different VLPO neurons exist, and
they differ in where they project; VLPO projects towards the tubero-
mammillary nucleus, and the VLPOe projects towards the locus coeruleus
(LC) and the raphe nuclei. The interaction between VLPOe and arousal
system functions as a switch, which is stabilized by orexin neurons [6].
10
2.5
The development from acute to chronic insomnia [6]. Acute insomnia
last for 1-90 days, subchronic insomnia last for 3-6 months, and chronic
insomnia last for more than 6 months. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .

CHAPTER 1
INTRODUCTION
Good sleep is central for health and normal cognitive functioning [1], since poor sleep or
sleep loss will have a negative effect on a person’s well-being. Studies have shown that
a short sleep duration and the correlated sleep disorder, e.g. insomnia, results in lower
happiness. Sequelae of chronic sleep loss includes diabetes, cardiovascular diseases,
and reduced capacity of managing daily tasks [2]. For diagnosing sleep disorders, dif-
ferent procedures can be used, but often a polysomnographic (PSG) test is conducted. A
PSG procedure consists of different physiological signals, including electroencephalog-
raphy (EEG), electromyography (EMG), electrooculography (EOG) and electrocardio-
graphy (ECG) [1], [3], [4]. However, it is not required to perform a PSG test to diagnose
e.g. insomnia, unless disorders such as sleep apnea are suspected [3]. The PSG data are
manually scored by sleep experts, who ﬁnd different sleep patterns for diagnosing the
patient [4].
Present, the insomnia diagnosis is based on patient complaints, regarding their sleep
quality frustrations [5], [6]. The diagnostic criteria is located in the Diagnostic and
Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders - 5th Edition (DSM-5) and the International
Classiﬁcation of Sleep Disorders - 3rd Edition (ICSD-3). The criteria does not include
objective sleep quality, due to lack of consistent validated deﬁned sleep parameters [5].
Based on the diagnostic criteria, the insomnia prevalence is as high as to 50-60%, de-
pending on the population and criteria applied [6]–[8].
In PSG studies of insomnia patients compared to healthy subjects, small differences
have been observed.
These differences included decreased total sleep time (TST),
slightly increased sleep onset latency (SOL), increased amount of nocturnal awaken-
ings, and increased wakefulness after sleep onset (WASO) [5], [9]. These ﬁndings are
more pronounced in subjective assessment, indicating that a deeper understanding of
the discrepancy between objective and subjective sleep parameters is essential [5], [6].
It is proposed that this discrepancy can be explained by patients misperceiving sleep
as wake, increasing attention on sleep-related threats, and increasing amount of short
awakenings during the night, called arousals [5].
1
==PAGE:32==
1.1. PROJECT DESCRIPTION
Chapter 1. Introduction
Arousals can be detected in EEG signals, and are deﬁned in the American Academy of
Sleep Medicine (AASM) as an abrupt shift in EEG frequency for more than 3 seconds,
with a minimum 10 seconds of previously stable sleep [10]. During the sleep stage
dubbed Rapid Eye Movement (REM) sleep, arousals also require an increase in the
submental EMG for at least 1 second. Patients with insomnia experience an increased
amount of arousals, resulting in a fragmented sleep process, which prevents restful sleep
and results in daytime sleepiness [1], [4].
The fragmented sleep, and hence poor sleep and sleep loss, results in an overall poor life
quality, with depression and increased accidents [8], [11]. These symptoms and the high
prevalence, drive researchers to gain more knowledge about the disease and its possible
treatment methods. The understanding of the insomnia pathophysiology is difﬁcult, due
to the not fully understood complex psychoneurobiology. Recently, hyperarousal and
stress dysregulation models of insomnia have provided essential insight into the disease
process [12].
The purpose of this thesis, is therefore to investigate different features, both arousal
speciﬁc and more general sleep features, in order to gain more insight into the disease.
1.1
Project Description
The basic understanding of the sleep-wake processor is inadequate; an unmet need is
the understanding of the relation of presence of macro-sleep, despite the perception of
poor, light or inadequate sleep. To understand the background of this hypothesis, the
hyperarousal theory implies; 1) that the brain can be in different states at the same time,
e.g. asleep with the perception of awake state, and 2) that the arousal system is dynamic
with signiﬁcant variation in the time domain. The author will aim to identify the sponta-
neous arousability in a dynamic sleep-wake process in patients with primary insomnia,
and compare to normal sleep pattern from control subjects.
The project aims to evaluate a current automated arousal detector, on its reliability to
detect spontaneous arousals in insomnia. Three middle-sized datasets of high-density
EEG will be used to validate the detector1 in an insomnia context. The main project
aim is to use the automatically annotated datasets for analysis of arousal morphology,
in order to discover physiological features that are speciﬁc for insomnia patients.
1The detector will be determined in Section 3.3.
2
==PAGE:33==
Chapter 1. Introduction
1.2. PROJECT GOALS
1.2
Project Goals
In order to discover speciﬁc physiological features, different analysis methods will be
used, such as:
• Extract features in multiple domains, e.g. time and frequency
• Investigate correlation between sleep stages and arousals, both frequency and
morphology
• Investigate connection between arousal linked to respiratory events and insomnia
1.3
Scope of the Thesis
The work conducted throughout this thesis is documented in this report, and in a paper
draft, which can be found in Appendix A. The second chapter describes the medical
background, necessary for understanding the insomnia disease, and assist as a reference
for the remaining chapters. The third chapter covers a literature study of the state of
the art work within insomnia research and arousal detection. This affects and guides the
choice of approach in this thesis, by summarizing the results and approaches achieved by
other researchers.The fourth chapter describes the data acquisition, the arousal detector
chosen in the previous chapter, and the preprocessing steps. The ﬁfth chapter describes
the feature extraction, the mathematical calculation, and the method used for signiﬁ-
cant testing. The sixth chapter presents the results of the insomnia research and the
signiﬁcant difference. The seventh chapter discusses the results obtained, and how they
correlate with the state of the art and the theory used. The eighth chapter summarizes
the ﬁndings of this thesis. Lastly, the ninth chapter discusses ideas for improvements,
and future research areas.
1.4
Practical Implementation
The feature extraction method will be conducted in the program called MATLAB. All
relevant scripts will be provided in Appendix E with detailed headers and comments, to
simplify the understanding of the programming steps for external readers.

CHAPTER 2
MEDICAL BACKGROUND OF INSOMNIA
This chapter provides knowledge important for understanding the insomnia disease,
and introduces the clinical terminology used in this thesis. First, the anatomy of the
brain is explained, to better understand the following topics. Then the basics of EEG
measurements, its technique, and brain waves are explained. This is followed by a
description of sleep, the sleep-wake regulation, and the brain area involved with sleep.
Finally, a thorough explanation regarding the insomnia disease is provided, including
the deﬁnition, diagnosis, clinical ﬁndings, and the pathophysiology. The descriptions
are provided at an adequate level, for the understanding for biomedical engineers.
2.1
The Brain
The brain is a highly complex organ, which can be divided into four main regions; cere-
brum, diencephalon, brainstem, and cerebellum, see Figure 2.1a. The four regions can
be further divided into several other brain regions, which will be described below.
The cerebrum is the largest brain region, which is divided into left and right hemi-
spheres, and further divided into lobes, see Figure 2.1b. The frontal lobe controls vol-
untary motor functions, motivation, mood etc. The parietal lobe receives and perceives
sensory information, such as touch, temperature and balance. The occipital lobe re-
ceives and perceives visual information. And ﬁnally, the temporal lobe is involved in
olfactory (smell), auditory sensation, memory, abstract thought, and judgment [13].
The diencephalon is located between the brainstem and the cerebrum, and can be di-
vided into the thalamus, the epithalamus, and the hypothalamus. The thalamus receives
sensory inputs from the spinal cord and brainstem, and sends the information to the
cerebral cortex. The mood inﬂuence and registration of unlocalized pain occurs in the
thalamus as well. The epithalamus is involved in odor response, and consists of the
pineal gland, which inﬂuences the puberty onset. The hypothalamus maintains home-
ostasis, including body temperature, hunger, thirst, fear etc., as well as controlling hor-
mone secretion from the pituitary gland, involved in emotional response to odors, and
5
==PAGE:36==
2.1. THE BRAIN
Chapter 2. Medical Background of Insomnia
is involved in memory [13].
The brainstem is the connection between the brain and the spinal cord, and it controls
several vital functions, e.g. heart rate, blood pressure, breathing, consciousness, sleep-
wake and circadian regulation. The latter three are together with the mid- and forebrain.
Due to this vital control, damage to small areas can be highly critical, and even fatal.
The brainstem can be divided into the medulla oblongata, the pons, and the midbrain.
The medulla oblongata regulates heart rate, blood vessel constriction, and breathing,
whereas the pons communicates between the cerebrum and cerebellum, and controls
chewing and salivation. Some nerves from the medulla oblongata extends into the pons,
causing the lower part of the pons to control functions such as breathing. The midbrain
consists of four mounds called the colliculi, which are involved in auditory nerve path-
ways, visual reﬂexes, and in receiving of touch and auditory inputs. The midbrain also
controls eye movements, pupil diameter, and lens shape, and contains the substantia
nigra, which regulates general body movements. Besides these regions, the brainstem
also contains a group of nuclei called the reticular formation. These are a major com-
ponent of the reticular activating system, which is important for arousing, maintaining
consciousness, and regulating the sleep-wake-cycle [13].
The cerebellum is involved in maintaining balance and muscle tone, and in coordination
of ﬁne motor movements. This is conducted by a comparator, which evaluates data from
the motor cortex and the peripheral structures, hence it compares information regarding
the intended movement and the moving structures. If the data differs, the cerebellum
corrects the discrepancy, resulting in smooth and coordinated movements. The cerebel-
lum also works together with the cerebrum to learn motor skills. When both regions
have acquired the skill, the movements can be performed smoothly and automatically
[13].
(a) The brain regions [14].
(b) The brain lobes [15].
Figure 2.1: Illustration of the brain regions, lobes, and their location.
6
==PAGE:37==
Chapter 2. Medical Background of Insomnia
2.2. ELECTROENCEPHALOGRAM RECORDING
2.2
Electroencephalogram Recording
The brain activity can be recorded by attaching electrodes onto the scalp, resulting in
an EEG recording. The recorded activity can be marked as different brain wave types,
see Figure 2.2, due to different intensity, frequency, and conscious level. Beta waves
occur during intense mental activity, which in the beginning of sleep transfers into alpha
waves. Alpha waves are observed during wake, but in a quiet and resting state with eyes
closed. When the sleep gets deeper, ﬁrst delta and later theta waves occur. [13], [16].
Figure 2.2: The four different brain waves [17].[17]
2.2.1
Techniques
The EEG recording is a non-invasive procedure that measures the brain activity. The
activated brain cells, called neurons, produce a current ﬂow, meaning the resting mem-
brane state is changed, due to a stimulus which activates gated ion channels. When
this current change exceeds a certain threshold, an action potential is triggered, which
propagates along the neuron. The information between neurons is transferred through
synapses, hence transfers the action potentials from one neuron to the other. When a
stimulus is applied to the neuron, Na+ voltage-gated channels open, which results in
depolarization of the cell. If this depolarization exceeds the threshold, more Na+ chan-
nels open, resulting in a 600-fold increase in membrane permeability. This results in the
inside of the neuron being more positively charged than the outside, which opens K+
channels. When K+ leaves the neuron, it repolarizes the neuron to its resting membrane
potential. This current change is recorded by the EEG electrodes placed at the surface
of the skull, but as a summarized action potential from multiple neurons, due to the
electrode location [13], [16].
7
==PAGE:38==
2.3. SLEEP UNDERSTANDING
Chapter 2. Medical Background of Insomnia
To conduct an EEG recording, electrodes with conductive media, ampliﬁers, analog to
digital converter, and a recording device are needed. The recording device, e.g. a com-
puter, is used to store and display the data, which is converted from analog to digital
by the converter. The data is ampliﬁed, for accurate digitization and to comply with the
Nyquist theorem. There exist multiple different EEG electrodes, but often the Ag-AgCl
is used, which accurately records the very slow changes. Between the electrodes and
the skull, a conductive paste is applied, to ensure low contact impedance [16].
For standardization of electrode placement, International Federation in Electroencephalog-
raphy and Clinical Neurophysiology has created the international 10-20 electrode sys-
tem, see Figure 2.3. The electrodes are placed at 10% or 20% of the distance between
skull landmarks; the Nasion, preauricular points, and the Inion. Each electrode is given
a letter, according to the adjacent brain area, and a number. The number indicates the
distance from the midline, indicated by Z, and which hemisphere the electrode is placed
on; odd numbers for the left, and even numbers for the right side. The international
electrode system consists of 21 electrodes, but if a higher spatial resolution is desired,
more electrodes can be added.
Figure 2.3: The international 10-20 electrode system for placement of the 21 electrodes
[16].
2.3
Sleep Understanding
Sleep is essential for survival, since it maintains the brain pathways responsible for
learning, memorizing, it ensures functions such as concentration and quick response,
is important for nerve cell communication, and is a part of toxin removal in the brain.
Almost all tissue types and body systems are affected by sleep. This explains why a
chronic lack of sleep, or poor sleep quality, increase the risk of different disorders, such
8
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 2. Medical Background of Insomnia
2.3. SLEEP UNDERSTANDING
as high blood pressure, cardiovascular disease, depression, and obesity [18].
Since multiple functions are affected by sleep, several structures of the brain are in-
volved. Within the hypothalamus, a group of nerve cells, called the suprachiasmatic
nucleus (SCN), affect sleep and arousal. The SCN receives information regarding the
light-dark cycle, and based on this, regulates the behavioral rhythm and sleep/wake
cycle. The brain stem in collaboration with the hypothalamus, administers the sleep-
/wake transitions, and produce the chemical gamma-Aminobutyric acid, which reduces
the arousal activity. During REM sleep, the brain stem relaxes muscles, to ensure that
dreams are not acted out. The thalamus is active during REM sleep, sending the cortex
images, sounds, and other sensations, to make the dreams feel more real [18].
For evaluation of the sleep, a PSG recording is performed. The PSG contains EEG,
EMG, EOG, ECG, airﬂow sensors, respiratory effort signals, oxygen saturation, and
body position sensors. After the recording, a sleep specialist scores the EEG data into
different sleep stages. There are three main stages; wake, REM and non-REM (NREM)
sleep, where the latter can be divided into three stages, according to the AASM standard.
For scoring of each sleep stage, different criteria need to be met [10]. After the scoring,
different sleep information can be extracted, such as time spend in each stage, number
of awakenings, and unusual brain activity patterns. Based on this information, and the
clinical history, a sleep disorder diagnosis can be made.
2.3.1
The Sleep-Wake Regulation
The circadian rhythm and the homeostatic drive regulates the sleep-wake cycle. The
SCN sets the circadian rhythm, and communicates the release of melatonin to the pineal
gland, which promotes sleep. The SCN is synchronized by external factors, such as
light. Light inhibits the melatonin release, and hence the day-night cycle affects the
sleep-wake cycle. Adenosine is produced during wakefulness, and the level increases
with prolonged periods of wake. Therefore, the homeostatic process refers to the in-
creased sleepiness with increased wakefulness [19].
2.3.2
Reticular Activating System
The ascending reticular activating system originates from the brainstem, and plays an
important role in cortical arousal, and in regulation of the sleep-wake states, see Fig-
ure 2.4. The wake state occurs, when a network of cells in the hypothalamus, called
encompassing orexinergic neurons, activates the thalamus and cerebral cortex. This
arousal system can be shut off during sleep, by the hypothalamus’ "key switch", which
is stabilized by hypothalamic neurons. A reduced amount of orexin neurons, results
9
==PAGE:40==
2.3. SLEEP UNDERSTANDING
Chapter 2. Medical Background of Insomnia
in an inappropriate state switching, which is seen in e.g. narcolepsy. This dysfunc-
tional "key switch" is suggested to be involved in the pathogenesis of PI. Since an im-
balance between the ventrolateral preoptic nucleus (VLPO) and gamma-Aminobutyric
acid (sleep-promoting brain areas), and an overactivity of the orexin system (arousal-
promoting neurons) or a hypofunction of the VLPO [6].
Based on a rat study it has been hypothesized, that when sleep circuitry shows sleep
activation, and the cortex and arousal system shows awake activation, it is due to a fully
activated VLPO, homeostatic and circadian pressure, and an active arousal system, ex-
cited by the limbic system. This resembles insomnia in humans, with the subjective
experience of daytime fatigue, due to insufﬁcient sleep, the inability to de-arouse dur-
ing sleep intending, and the sleep state misperception [6].
Figure 2.4: The "key switch" of the sleep-wake regulation. During sleep, VLPO neurons
are active, and hence a loss of VLPO neurons results in fragmented sleep and insom-
nia. Two different VLPO neurons exist, and they differ in where they project; VLPO
projects towards the tuberomammillary nucleus, and the VLPOe projects towards the
locus coeruleus (LC) and the raphe nuclei. The interaction between VLPOe and arousal
system functions as a switch, which is stabilized by orexin neurons [6].
10
==PAGE:41==
Chapter 2. Medical Background of Insomnia
2.4. INSOMNIA
2.4
Insomnia
A common misconception is that insomnia is only a symptom, and not a disease that
requires its own treatment. However, insomnia can be either a symptom or a disorder.
The insomnia symptoms include complaints of difﬁculty falling asleep, where the dis-
order includes the sleep complaints together with a signiﬁcant distress and functional
impairment. The insomnia diagnosis can be made, when one or more of the following
problems are present [20]:
• Hard time initiating sleep
• Struggle to maintain sleep, and waking up frequently during the night
• Wake up too early and being unable to go back to sleep
• The sleep is nonrestorative, or of poor quality
The insomnia disorder can be classiﬁed into different subtypes, and this classiﬁcation
depends on the manual used. The DSM have three subtypes; PI, insomnia related to
medical or mental disease, and insomnia related to the intake or abuse from substances.
The ICSD have 11 subtypes, including acute, psychophysiological, paradoxical, idio-
pathic, and substance-induced insomnia. They also classify insomnia as chronic, if
symptoms have been present for more than 6 months [3], [6].
The prevalence for chronic insomnia is estimated to range between 9-12% in adults,
up to 20% in old age, and is twice as common for women. Due to the high prevalence,
sleep disturbances are a common complaint throughout the population, and once insom-
nia manifests, it may persist over several years. Studies have revealed an increased risk
of depression, panic disorder (such as anxiety), obsessive compulsive disorder, and ad-
dictions among subjects with preexisting insomnia. Therefore, it is not surprising, that
these patients more commonly have a dependency on alcohol, hypnotic, and sedating
drugs. Accordingly, insomnia is a considerable public health problem [3], [6].
Despite that, research has revealed a remarkable disagreement between subjective ex-
perience and a rather undisturbed polysomnographic sleep. Hence PSG studies have
not become a standard procedure for diagnosing insomnia, it is only required, if dis-
orders such as sleep apnea are suspected. The diagnose is based on subjective sleep
questionnaires, assessing the sleep behavior, and on sleep diaries. Hence it is based on
complaints from the patient, regarding prolonged SL, difﬁculty to maintain sleep, expe-
rience of non-refreshing, or poor sleep, in combination with impaired daytime function-
ing. The impaired functioning includes reduced alertness, fatigue, and exhaustion. To
be diagnosed with insomnia, these symptoms have to be present, for at least four weeks,
11
==PAGE:42==
2.4. INSOMNIA
Chapter 2. Medical Background of Insomnia
according to the DSM [3], [6].
Once diagnosed, the AASM has published different treatment strategies for insomnia.
A recommendation is created, to help clinicians decide which pharmacological agent
to use. The recommendation suggest, whether the individual drug, should be used for
treating the speciﬁc insomnia type. The recommendation includes 14 individual agents,
including prescription medication (e.g. zolpidem), over-the-counter medication (de-
phenhydramine), and dietary supplements (melatonin) [21]. Recommendation regard-
ing non-pharmacological treatment of insomnia, is based on meta-analysis that demon-
strates the cognitive-behavioral treatment (CBT) efﬁciency. Studies focusing on clinical
effectiveness have shown that CBT, with or without pharmacotherapy, are preferable
over pharmacotherapy alone for older adults. Resulting in CBT being the ﬁrst choice
of treatment for chronic insomnia. Hypnotic medication has also been studied, but lim-
ited evidence has been provided for long-term effects. It is known that difﬁculty falling
asleep, can be a results of stimuli incompatible with sleep, and that the patient has not
succeeded in establishing a discriminative stimulus for sleep. Therefore, insomniacs try
a stimulus control treatment, which instructs to only lay down when sleepy, only using
the bed for sleeping (sexual activity excepted), get out of bed if they cannot fall asleep
within 15-20 minutes, get out of bed at the same time every day, and avoiding daytime
napping [3].
2.4.1
Clinical Findings
PSG studies of patients diagnosed with insomnia compared to good sleepers, have
shown less pronounced differences than expected, based on the subjective assessments.
However, most studies found a signiﬁcantly prolonged SOL, increased WASO, and re-
duced SE in insomnia patients. The most consistent ﬁndings, are a smaller PSG differ-
ence magnitude, compared to subjectively estimated differences [6].
Some studies have found an increased heart rate during presleep and nighttime in in-
somniacs, however, other studies have failed to replicate the results. Few studies have
investigated an elevated core body temperature as a hyperarousal marker, which seems
to be elevated in elderly insomnia patients during nighttime. Regrettably, most of these
studies could not be replicated, uses small sample sizes, and uses a wide variety of in-
somnia deﬁnitions, resulting in a lack of a clear conclusion [6].
Studies have suggested an enhanced autonomic arousal in poor sleepers, both before
and during sleep, which increases rectal temperature, number of vasoconstrictions per
minutes, perspiration rate, skin conductance and number of body movements per hour.
It has also been suggested, that high levels of hormones indicate adreno-cortical activity,
12
==PAGE:43==
Chapter 2. Medical Background of Insomnia
2.4. INSOMNIA
and that insomniacs experience a less rapid heart rate decline during sleep [3].
Insomnia patients complain about different neuropsychological domains, e.g. alertness
and memory functioning, but studies have failed to detect any robust impairments. A
night-to-night variability in some insomniacs have been found, which further compli-
cates the task of investigating group differences [6]. All studies on insomniacs have both
been supported and denied by other researchers, making it difﬁcult to fully understand
the pathophysiology.
2.4.2
Pathophysiology
It is known that sleep is a complex system, which is regulated by multiple genes, gene
interactions, environment, and gene-environment interactions. Due to this, it is unlikely
that insomnia is caused by a single gene, but more likely multiple mechanisms including
psychologic, comorbid, psychiatric, medical, neurologic, pharmacologic, sleep misper-
ception etc. This results in a physiological predisposed risk or vulnerability of insomnia,
which will be triggered under environmental circumstances, such as stress and conﬂicts.
Studies have found that the insomnia onset, was related with stress or life changes [3],
[6].
The DSM and ICSD have reported a familial association with light, disrupted sleep
within insomnia patients. ICSD also reports that an anxious, overconcerned behavior
regarding health, could be a predisposing factor, indicating that insomniacs appear prone
to worrying. Elevated autonomic and metabolic rates are suggested as a vulnerability
factor for insomnia as well. It is unlikely, that these predisposed factors alone can create
the imbalance in sleep homeostasis or circadian timing seen in insomnia, but they may
impair sleep quality and induce sleep state misperception [3].
The hyperarousal theory
The pathophysiology of insomnia, particularly PI and psychophysiological, is based on
an interaction between psychological and physiological factors. The hyperarousal the-
ory is therefore an integrative approach to the pathophysiology. It suggests that patients
who ruminate about their sleep complaints, are susceptible for developing "learned sleep
preventing associations", which explains the chronicity of the disorder. Other behaviors
are also suggested to affect the presence of insomnia, such as daytime napping and in-
creased alcohol consumption. Acute insomnia is triggered by different stressors, and
often the sleep complaints resolve, when removing the stressors again. However, some
13
==PAGE:44==
2.4. INSOMNIA
Chapter 2. Medical Background of Insomnia
develop chronic insomnia, which is independent of the stressors, see Figure 2.5 [6].
A perpetuating factor in chronic insomnia can be arousals. Arousals are deﬁned as an
abrupt shift of EEG frequency of more than 16 Hz, lasting for at least 3 seconds, and
are preceding at least 10 seconds stable sleep. The arousals are scored using the EEG
channels, but in REM sleep, an EMG channel is required as well [10]. The arousals
can be expressed in terms of somatic, cognitive, or cortical activation, where the bed
and sleep environment acts as stimuli. Cortical arousal, which manifests as increased
frequencies in the EEG, results in irregular levels of sensory and information process-
ing, as well as long-term memory formation. These arousals are associated with sleep
continuity disturbances and/or sleep state misperception. An increased sensory process-
ing results in increased vulnerability to environmental stimuli, which affects the sleep
initiation and/or maintenance. The increased information processing affects the distinc-
tion between sleep and wakefulness, resulting in the perception of sleep as wake. An
increased memory formation, of events related to SO and arousals, affect the subjective
interpretation of sound and uninterrupted sleep. These increased factors are expected to
correlate with altered SL, WASO, and sleep duration [6].
Figure 2.5: The development from acute to chronic insomnia [6]. Acute insomnia last
for 1-90 days, subchronic insomnia last for 3-6 months, and chronic insomnia last for
more than 6 months.
14
==PAGE:45==
CHAPTER 3
STATE OF THE ART
This chapter covers a literature study, which was conducted to ensure that the author
gained an overview of the newest research within insomnia, arousal characteristics, and
arousal detection. The literature study was accomplished, to ensure that this project
would further evolve the research area, by providing novel information. First, a thor-
ough description of top 10 studies within insomnia research are provided, followed by
a description of other insomnia studies. Then a comparison between different arousal
detectors are provided. Finally, a summary of the knowledge gained within this chapter,
and a decision regarding which approach and features to implement within this thesis,
is provided. The research articles presented in this chapter, have been selected from a
comprehensive literature search, which is further described in Appendix B.
3.1
Method Used in Insomnia Studies
Sleep Stage Transition Dynamics Reveal Speciﬁc Stage 2 Vulnerability in Insomnia
Authors: Yishul Wei, Michele A. Colombo, Jennifer R. Ramautar, Tessa F. Blanken, Ys-
brand D. van der Werf, Kai Spiegelhalder, Bernd Feige, Dieter Riemann, Eus J. W. Van
Someren [22]
The purpose of this study, was to evaluate possible sleep stage dynamic differences
between insomnia patients and matched controls, and whether these features
differentiated better than conventional sleep parameters.
Method: 46 patients with insomnia (age 23-69 years) and 42 age- and sex-matched
controls were recruited. The sleep was scored visually by an expert. Analysis of con-
ventional sleep parameters included TST, SOL, WASO, SE, and number of awakenings.
The probability of sleep stage transition, was referred to as the sleep stages dynamics.
The group differences were evaluated using Wilcoxon rank-sum tests. The proposed
method is schematically illustrated in Figure 3.1.
15
==PAGE:46==
3.1. METHOD USED IN INSOMNIA STUDIES
Chapter 3. State of the Art
Figure 3.1: Schematic illustration of the sleep stage transition method.
Results: A signiﬁcant increase in Wake, WASO, and amount of stage N1, and a sig-
niﬁcant decrease in TST, SE, and amount of stage N3 for insomnia was found. Insom-
nia patients had a signiﬁcant increased probability, of stage N2 to N1 transition, and a
higher probability of stage N2 to Wake transition. All parameters showed the same in-
crease/decrease tendency in the validation dataset, but were different in their magnitude
and signiﬁcance.
Discussion/Conclusion: In spite of the signiﬁcant stage transition, the ﬁndings indi-
cated that stage N3 was normal in insomnia patients, and they remain normal in this
stage.
Comments: It was a good idea to validate the ﬁndings on another data set, in order
to test the generalization, and to remove the data speciﬁc ﬁndings. Not providing the
preprocessing steps, results in the study not being reproducible, which is not acceptable.
Does REM sleep contribute to subjective wake time in primary insomnia? A com-
parison of polysomnographic and subjective sleep in 100 patients
Authors: Bernd Feige, Anam Al-Shajlawi, Christoph Nissen, Ulrich Voderholzer, Mag-
dolna Hornyak, Kai Spiegelhalder, Corinna Kloepfer, Michael Perlis and Dieter Rie-
mann [23]
The purpose of this study was to relate PSG characteristics to subjectively reported
wake times.
Method: 56 patients (mean age 40.91± 12.83 years) with PI and 56 age-matched good
sleeper controls had subjective sleep data and PSG recordings. The recordings were
evaluated for TST, SE, SOL, arousal index (evaluated both within TST and speciﬁc
stages), and amount of sleep stages. The REM variables were REM latency and REM
density. These parameters were compared to the subjective parameters, based on ques-
tionnaires from the participants. For parameter comparison, the Chi-squared tests were
16
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 3. State of the Art
3.1. METHOD USED IN INSOMNIA STUDIES
used, based on Wilk’s Lamba. The proposed method is schematically illustrated in Fig-
ure 3.2.
Figure 3.2: Schematic illustration of the objective versus subjective parameter compar-
ison method.
Results: PI had increased number of awakenings, and arousal index, as well as de-
creased TST, REM sleep, and SE compared to controls. The number of eye movements
in REM sleep were signiﬁcantly reduced in PI, but not the REM density, possibly due to
a lower amount of REM sleep. PI subjects had signiﬁcantly worse reported sleep, and
signiﬁcantly higher negative sleep perception. Both groups overestimated TST more of-
ten than underestimated, resulting in subjective wake time being best modeled by wake
time and REM time from PSG.
Discussion/Conclusion: The analysis showed that PI patients had both PSG and sub-
jective impairments of sleep. The PSG wake time, with consideration of PSG REM
time, was nearly identical with the subjective wake time for both groups. The PI group
reported more wake time per minute of REM sleep, indicating a fundamentally modi-
ﬁed REM sleep quality.
Comments: It was a good idea to compare the PSG ﬁndings with the subjective data,
in order to be able to diagnose the disease with more objective data in the future. To
analyse the data within different sleep stages, instead of only as a combination is good,
since the different sleep stages have different appearances and physiological functions.
Not providing the preprocessing steps, results in the study not being reproducible, which
is not acceptable.
Data-Driven Analysis of EEG Reveals Concomitant Superﬁcial Sleep During Deep
Sleep in Insomnia Disorder
Authors: Julie Anja Engelhard Christensen, Rick Wassing, Yishul Wei, Jennifer R. Ra-
mautar, Oti Lakbila-Kamal, Poul Jørgen Jennum and Eus J. W. Van Someren [24]
17
==PAGE:48==
3.1. METHOD USED IN INSOMNIA STUDIES
Chapter 3. State of the Art
The purpose of this study was to detect group differences between insomnia and
healthy subjects.
Method: 55 participants with insomnia (mean age 47.8 ± 12.9 years) and 64 healthy
participants were recruited. All participants underwent EEG recording and the data was
scored according to the AASM standard, and analyzed using an automated topic mod-
eling (previously validated). The procedure described each epoch as a mixture of vig-
ilance states, instead of the traditional single sleep stages. All 1 second data bins were
Fast Fourier Transformed to obtain the spectral power in a subject-speciﬁc distribution.
Afterwards, the data bins were given a distribution classiﬁcations letter; Extreme (E),
High (H), Median (M), Low (L) or Very low (V). The observed word patterns were com-
pared to the six expected word patterns for each topic (T1-T6), and the topic with the
highest probability could be related to a speciﬁc sleep stage. The Wilcoxon rank-sum
test was used to evaluate the group differences. The proposed method is schematically
illustrated in Figure 3.3.
Figure 3.3: Schematic illustration of the topic model method.
Results: Insomniacs had signiﬁcantly higher insomnia severity, and signiﬁcantly shorter
total sleep duration. There was no signiﬁcant group difference for the six topics. In
epoch with T4 dominance (corresponds to N1 sleep), insomnia patients had a more
dominant T4 compared to controls, and for epochs dominated by T1 (corresponds to
very deep N3 sleep), insomnia patients had a signiﬁcantly higher co-occurrence of T4,
indicating that insomnia patients switch from deep sleep to lighter sleep or wakefulness,
more often than controls.
Discussion/Conclusion: It is shown that insomnia patients have more light sleep, which
suggests the presence of hyperarousal during insomnia patients deepest sleep.
Comments: It was a good idea to check for light sleep features during deep sleep, in or-
der to explain the insomnia complaints. However, only a limited amount of participants
were included, and a generalization of the results cannot be made.
18
==PAGE:49==
Chapter 3. State of the Art
3.1. METHOD USED IN INSOMNIA STUDIES
Alpha-wave frequency characteristics in health and insomnia during sleep
Authors: Justus T. C. Schwabedal, Maik Riedl, Thomas Penzel and Niels Wessel [25]
The purpose of this study was to explore the dynamics of the alpha waves.
Figure 3.4: Schematic illustration of the alpha wave frequency model method.
Results: A signiﬁcant group difference was found for stages wake, S1, S3, S4, TST,
and SE. Insomnia patients had signiﬁcant alpha frequency reduction from wake to WA-
SOs, but the alpha variability increased when the duration of wake episodes decreased.
The alpha variability was signiﬁcantly increased from wake to arousals for insomnia
patients, and the alpha frequency was signiﬁcantly increased in arousals compared to
short WASOs.
Discussion/Conclusion: The transition to sleep was marked by ﬂuctuations in the al-
pha oscillation amplitude during resting wakefulness, and this alpha decay was possibly
related to alpha desynchronization or destabilization. According to the hyperarousal
theory, insomnia interfered with this process. The arousal alpha frequency was similar
to those in wakefulness for insomnia patients but not in controls, which could indicate
a microstate of increased consciousness.
Comments: It can be difﬁcult to compare results from two groups that signiﬁcantly
differ in age, however, they argue well about why it did not affect the results. They use
a small number of male only participants, which makes a generalization of the results
difﬁcult.
Alpha-wave Characteristics in Psychophysiological Insomnia
Authors: Mohammad Rezaei, Hiwa Mohammadi and Habibolah Khazaie [26]
The purpose of this study was to characterize alpha variability in Psychophysiological
Insomnia.
Method: 11 Psychophysiological insomnia (Psych-Insomnia) patients (mean age 44 ±
13.27 years) and 11 matched healthy subjects were recruited. All participants answered
19
==PAGE:50==
3.1. METHOD USED IN INSOMNIA STUDIES
Chapter 3. State of the Art
a questionnaire, and had a PSG performed. The PSG data was divided into 4 conditions,
wake before SO, WASOs, REM, and NREM. The power spectral density was calculated
by Fourier Transformation of epochs in all four stages, in order to obtain alpha power, -
frequency and their variability. The group comparison of alpha characteristics was done
by independent-sample t-testing. The proposed method is schematically illustrated in
Figure 3.5.
Figure 3.5: Schematic illustration of the alpha wave characteristics method.
Results: A signiﬁcant group difference was found for subjective assessment of sleep
quality, TST, SE, sleep latency (SL), daytime dysfunction, and PSQI sleep quality, as
well as for objective percent of sleep stages (expect REM). The alpha power amplitude
was signiﬁcantly different in REM and NREM, and the power variability was signiﬁ-
cantly different in NREM. The alpha frequency variability was signiﬁcant in REM and
NREM.
Discussion/Conclusion: During wake before SO, the alpha activity and variability were
increased for Psych-Insomnia patients, which could be explained by the decrease and
increase of alpha activity intensity. Psych-Insomnia patients showed increased alpha
activity and variability before initiating sleep, but after initiating sleep it was reversed.
This could be explained, by different brain activity between the right and left regions,
or during the sleep initiation, an activity change in the two hemispheres occur.
Comments: The study has a small sample size, which makes the generalization of the
results difﬁcult. Since the study had the subjective assessments, a comparison to the
objective assessment would have been nice.
Wake High-Density Electroencephalographic Spatiospectral Signatures of Insom-
nia
Authors: Michele A. Colombo, Jennifer R. Ramautar, Yishul Wei, Germán Gomez-
Herrero, Diederick Stoffers, Rick Wassing, Jeroen S. Benjamins, Enzo Tagliazucchi,
20
==PAGE:51==
Chapter 3. State of the Art
3.1. METHOD USED IN INSOMNIA STUDIES
Ysbrand D. van der Werf, Christian Cajochen and Eus J.W. Van Someren [27]
The purpose of this study was to investigate if physiological insomnia sleep
characteristics can be found during wake.
Method: 51 participants with insomnia (mean age 50.0 ± 13.4 years) and 43 matched
healthy control subjects were recruited, and all had answered a 7-day sleep diary for
subjective assessment of sleep. During the EEG recording, between 19:15 and 23:45,
the ﬁrst ﬁve minutes, participants were visually ﬁxated on a crosshair, and afterwards
had their eyes closed for ﬁve minutes, resulting in two wake resting-state conditions.
The relative spectral power was estimated for all electrodes signals, by dividing the
frequencies into 116 bins between 1.5 Hz and 40 Hz, in order to enhance topographi-
cal frequency differences. A ratio (LO/HI ratio) between lower (1.5-16 Hz) and upper
(16-40 Hz) spectrum parts was calculated. The group difference was evaluated using
Wilcoxon rank sum test. The proposed method is schematically illustrated in Figure
3.6.
Figure 3.6: Schematic illustration of the waking insomnia signature method.
Results: Insomnia patients had signiﬁcantly longer SOL and WASO, shorter TST, lower
SE, earlier lights-on time, and shorter time in bed (TIB). The average LO/HI ratio was
signiﬁcantly lower for insomnia patients during eyes closed, and decreased during eyes
open.
Discussion/Conclusion: During eyes open and closed, insomnia patients had a spectral
energy shift from low to high frequency range, according to the LO/HI ratio, due to an
altered balance of the cortical dynamics.
Comments: The analysis is based on a small amount of EEG data, and to make it more
generalized, several days could have been included.
21
==PAGE:52==
3.1. METHOD USED IN INSOMNIA STUDIES
Chapter 3. State of the Art
Hyperarousal during sleep in untreated, major depressed subjects with prodromal
insomnia: A polysomnographic study
Authors: Matthieu Heina, Christelle Senterreb, Jean-Pol Lanquarta, Xavier Montanaa,
Gwénolé Loasa, Paul Linkowskia and Philippe Hubaina [28]
The purpose of this study was to investigate polysomnigraphic dynamics, related to
hyperarousal in patients with major depression and insomnia.
Method: 66 patients with major depression (PMD) with prodromal insomnia, 86 pa-
tients with PI (aged 18-65 years) and 30 healthy matched controls were recruited. All
participants had answered questionnaires regarding e.g. their sleep quality, insomnia
symptoms and daytime sleepiness. The PSG recording was performed, and specialised
technicians scored the sleep stages. The PSG data were divided into three equally sized
segments, wherein the percentages of stage 1, stage 2, SWS (stage 3 + stage 4), REM
and WASO were calculated. Based on the PSG data, SL, sleep period time (SPT), TST,
SE and number of cycles were calculated. A non-parametric Kruskal-Wallis test was
used to test group difference signiﬁcance. The proposed method is schematically illus-
trated in Figure 3.7.
Figure 3.7: Schematic illustration of the polysomnographic dynamics method.
Results: Patients with PMD and PI had signiﬁcantly increased SL and WASO, and sig-
niﬁcantly decreased SE, SPT, duration of each segment, TST, and SWS/REM. PMD
and PI patients had decreased REM time in the ﬁrst and third segment, and decreased
SWS in the ﬁrst segment. A signiﬁcant negative correlation between WASO percentage
and REM, stage 2 and SWS percentages was found.
Discussion/Conclusion: Patients with PMD and with PI showed similar patterns, sug-
gesting that clinical depression onset depends on insomnia and hyperarousal. Hyper-
arousal mainly occurs in the SO period, and during the ﬁrst and third segment in PMD
and PI patients.

==PAGE:53==
Chapter 3. State of the Art
3.1. METHOD USED IN INSOMNIA STUDIES
Comments: They use a small sample size, which makes the generalization difﬁcult.
Even though they focus on PMD, they use PI as a comparison, and have some interesting
ideas about splitting the night into segments. Not providing the preprocessing steps,
results in the study not being reproducible, which is not acceptable.
Insomnia is Associated with Cortical Hyperarousal as Early as Adolescence
Authors: Julio Fernandez-Mendoza, Yun Li, Alexandros N. Vgontzas, Jidong Fang, Jor-
dan Gaines, Susan L. Calhoun, Duanping Liao and Edward O. Bixler [29]
The purpose of this study was to investigate spectral EEG high-frequency dynamics in
insomnia patients.
Method: 23 participants with insomnia and 21 healthy matched subjects were recruited,
all younger than 21 years old. All participants had a PSG recording done for nine hours,
which was scored by experienced raters. Based on the PSG data,TST, SE, WASO, and
percentages in each sleep stage were extracted.
Spectral analysis was performed on central EEG derivations, beta frequencies and four
sleep states (SOL, SO, NREM sleep and WASO). Relative power group differences
was estimated using multivariate analysis of covariance (MANCOVA). The proposed
method is schematically illustrated in Figure 3.8.
Figure 3.8: Schematic illustration of the spectral high-frequency dynamics method.
Results: A signiﬁcant increase of SOL, WASO and relative power in both low- and
high-beta frequency bands during SOL, SO, and NREM sleep, a signiﬁcant decrease
of sleep duration, and SE, and a marginally signiﬁcant difference during WASO was
seen in insomnia patients. The insomnia group was divided into subgroups of normal or
short sleep duration. The subgroup with short sleep duration, had signiﬁcantly increased
relative power in beta frequency bands during all sleep and wake states, where the sub-
group with normal sleep only had increased relative power during SOL, and marginally
increased during SO and NREM sleep.
23
==PAGE:54==
3.1. METHOD USED IN INSOMNIA STUDIES
Chapter 3. State of the Art
Discussion/Conclusion: The ﬁndings indicated that cortical hyperarousal was present
during adolescence for insomnia, since an increased beta frequency was detected during
attempting to fall asleep and NREM sleep. The ﬁndings also suggested that the degree
and patterns of these hyperarousals differed between insomnia subtypes, based on sleep
duration.
Comments: The limited amount of participants, makes generalization of the results
difﬁcult. Investigating insomnia subgroups could give useful information to understand
the disease and its severity better.
REM sleep as a potential indicator of hyperarousal in psychophysiological and
paradoxical insomnia sufferers
Authors: Alexandra D. Pérusse, Maude Pedneault-Drolet, Christine Rancourt, Isabelle
Turcotte, Geneviève St-Jean and Célyne H. Bastien [30]
The purpose of this study was to investigate hyperarousal in REM sleep for
psychophysiological and paradoxical insomnia patients.
Method: 39 Psych-Insomnia patients, 27 paradoxical insomnia (Para-Insomnia) pa-
tients and 47 healthy subjects were recruited, all aged between 25 and 55 years old.
Participants answered questionnaires to self evaluate their sleep difﬁculties. All partici-
pants had PSG recordings done for several nights, and were scored by experienced sleep
technicians. Different variables related to REM sleep, the number of eye movements,
density of EM, and arousal ratio were obtained. For group comparisons multivariate
ANCOVA was used, followed by Bonferroni post hoc analysis for signiﬁcance. The
proposed method is schematically illustrated in Figure 3.9.
Figure 3.9: Schematic illustration of the hyperarousal in REM sleep method.
Results: Signiﬁcant group difference between controls and insomniacs was found for
total wake time, WASO, and SE. Increased signiﬁcant group differences based on sub-
jective parameters were found for SOL, WASO, TWT, TST, and SE. Psych-Insomnia
had signiﬁcantly more wake intrusion during REM sleep compared to Para-Insomnia.
24
==PAGE:55==
Chapter 3. State of the Art
3.1. METHOD USED IN INSOMNIA STUDIES
For Para-Insomnia, analysis showed a correlation between REM sleep duration with SE,
TST and TWT, and between REM proportion with subjective SE and marginally with
TWT. A correlation between subjective SE and TST for Psych-Insomnia.
Discussion/Conclusion: The ﬁndings suggested that REM sleep was not an adequate
indicator of hyperarousal, this was contrary to previous studies, which showed de-
creased REMS, REM sleep duration, and REM proportion. This study had smaller
group difference of SE compared to previous studies, indicating that the greater the
group difference of discrepancy between SE was, the higher the chance was of observ-
ing difference in REM sleep.
Comments: The small sample size makes generalization of results difﬁcult. The idea to
base the analysis on more than one night is very good, in order to capture the variability.
Not providing the preprocessing steps, results in the study not being reproducible, which
is not acceptable.
How Hyperarousal and Sleep Reactivity Are Represented in Different Adult Age
Groups: Results from a Large Cohort Study on Insomnia
Authors: Ellemarije Altena, Ivy Y. Chen, Yannick Daviaux, Hans Ivers, Pierre Philip
and Charles M. Morin [31]
The purpose of this study was to investigate the relationship between hyperarousal and
sleep reactivity with insomnia severity.
Method: 1693 participants (aged 25-55 years) were recruited, and had to answer a
questionnaire regarding the ISI, the Arousal Predisposition Scale and the Ford Insomnia
Repose to Stress Test. The arousal and sleep reactivity could be analysed to assess how
insomnia vulnerability affected insomnia severity. The participants were divided into
four groups; 1) a high insomnia vulnerability group, with high scores for both Arousal
Predisposition Scale and Ford Insomnia Repose to Stress Test, 2) a low vulnerability
group, with low scores for both Arousal Predisposition Scale and Ford Insomnia Re-
pose to Stress Test, and 3-4) a medium vulnerability group, with a high score on one
and a low score on the other scale. The effect of age on sleep reactivity and arousal
was analysed in three age groups; 25-35 years old, 35-45 years old, and 45-55 years
old. In both parallel and across age, the group was divided into three sleep groups; good
sleepers, with insomnia symptoms, and without insomnia symptoms (subjects dissatis-
ﬁed with their sleep). For comparison a multi-factorial analysis of variance was used.
The proposed method is schematically illustrated in Figure 3.10.
25
==PAGE:56==
3.2. AROUSAL FEATURES
Chapter 3. State of the Art
Figure 3.10: Schematic illustration of the sleep reactivity method.
Results: The group divided based on reactivity scores, had a signiﬁcant group differ-
ence for insomnia severity. The vulnerability group division, showed a signiﬁcant main
group effect for ISI scores, and that most groups were signiﬁcant from each other, except
the two medium vulnerability groups. The division into age groups, had a signiﬁcant
decrease in arousal score from 25-35 years to 45-55 years. A group division of sleep/in-
somnia group, showed signiﬁcantly increased arousal and sleep reactivity scores from
good sleepers to insomnia symptoms to insomnia, which conﬁrms the insomnia vulner-
ability.
Discussion/Conclusion: The ﬁndings showed a strong association between arousal and
sleep reactivity with sleep problems, and that a high score was associated with high
insomnia severity level. This study showed an age dependent arousal decrease, which
indicates that hyperarousal plays a smaller role for insomnia in older subjects.
Comments: It is very interesting to divide the participants into several different groups,
in order to check for different association. This different group division provides useful
information for better understanding of the disease in different age groups. Not pro-
viding the preprocessing steps, results in the study not being reproducible, which is not
acceptable.
3.2
Arousal Features
Other studies have focused on different areas within insomnia and arousal research, and
to gain an overview of the newest research and knowledge within this ﬁeld, a short sum-
mary of different approaches will be explained.
M. Corsi-Cabrera et. al. in [32] have investigated waking EEG signals in 10 patients
with PI, and compared the results to 10 healthy controls. Only 2 minutes of EEG data
before lights off and at 10 am were used for analysis. A signiﬁcant decrease in beta and
gamma power from before and after sleep was found in controls. This was not found for
insomnia patients, they had a higher morning beta and gamma power. The difference in
beta and gamma power from night to morning, was signiﬁcantly negatively correlated
with the sleep quality, suggesting that a small decrease in beta and gamma powers are
26
==PAGE:57==
Chapter 3. State of the Art
3.2. AROUSAL FEATURES
associated with chronic poorer sleep quality.
F. Benz et. al. in [33] investigated the perceived wakefulness in 41 PI patients and 42
good sleeper controls, by waking the participants with a white noise sound, 5 minutes
after N2 SO and 5 minutes after REM SO. They found no signiﬁcant group differ-
ence between arousal number and REM density. However, a signiﬁcant difference of
the mean perceived wakefulness was found, which was associated with a signiﬁcantly
decreased delta, theta, and alpha power. The authors therefore hypothesized, that an
increased number of arousals, would be linked with higher perceived wakefulness.
S. Hernández-Torres et. al. in [34] investigated the sleep architecture in 46 children
with arousal disorder and 43 matched healthy controls. They found that children with
arousal disorder, had a signiﬁcant increased REM SL and percentage of REM sleep, as
well as a signiﬁcant decrease in percentages of light sleep. The signiﬁcant increase for
percentage of REM sleep, and a decrease of light sleep, could be a result of the CNS
maturity, which is known to result in decreased REM and N3 sleep, and increased light
sleep.
The frequency changes in insomnia patients using functional magnetic resonance imag-
ing (fMRI), have been studied by [35], [36] and [37].
F. Zhou et. al. in [35] uses 27 patients with PI and 27 healthy matched participants,
which are all scanned using a 3.0 T scanner. A standard T2*-weighted gradient echo se-
quence was used as a resting state fMRI (rs-fMRI) image. The signal frequency (0-0.25
Hz) was divided into ﬁve frequency bands by Fast Fourier Transformation. The aver-
aged square root power spectrum is called the amplitude of low-frequency ﬂuctuation
(ALFF). C. Li et. al. in [37] had 55 patients with PI and 44 matched healthy controls,
and uses the same method as F. Zhou et. al. but with a 1.5 T scanner instead. F. Zhou
et. al. found a signiﬁcant ALFF decrease in PI patients in 7 regions and a signiﬁcant
increase in 6 regions, where C. Li et. al. only found a signiﬁcant difference in 4 and 2
regions, respectively. They did not agree in the affected brain regions, but agree that the
decrease occurs in the temporal region and the increase occurs in the frontal region.
Y.-K. Wang et. al. in [36] uses 30 patients with PI and 15 healthy controls, and follow
the same method as F. Zhou et. al. but also calculates the fractional ALFF (fALFF).
They found an age-related fALFF difference in PI patients which was mainly located in
the cerebellum and temporal region. A signiﬁcant decrease in fALFF value was found
with increasing age. The gender-related fALFF difference in PI patients was located in
the temporal, posterior, and the middle frontal regions.
27
==PAGE:58==
3.3. AROUSAL DETECTOR
Chapter 3. State of the Art
3.3
Arousal Detector
Different arousal detectors have been proposed, in order to make the sleep evaluation
and diagnosing faster, and less receptive to subjectivity and errors. Some of these meth-
ods will be described and compared, see Table 3.1, in order to choose the best method
for arousal detection.
H. Abdullah et. al. in [38] extracted nonlinear features, based on epochs of 30 seconds,
such as largest lyapunov eponent (LLE), detrended ﬂuctuation analysis (DFA), and sam-
ple entropy. LLE is a measure of the amount of chaos in a signal, DFA is a measure of
long-range correlation in a non-stationary time series, and sample entropy is a measure
of regularity or self-similarity. Based on these features, the signal was classiﬁed based
on support vector machine (SVM). The classiﬁer got a sensitivity of 85%, speciﬁcity of
80%, and accuracy of 83%, based on three nonlinear features.
A. Brink-Kjaer et. al. in [39] had designed a convolutional neural network (CNN), to
compute signal features in 1 second bins for EEG, EOG and EMG. All signals were
resampled to 128 Hz, ﬁltered with an anti-aliasing and Butterworth ﬁlter, and an adap-
tive ﬁlter was applied to remove artifacts. The features are used to predict the label
probability of arousal, non-arousal, wake and sleep. The event detection is made in
post-processing steps, including probability threshold. The method achieved a preci-
sion, recall, and F1 score for the test set of 0.72, 0.81, and 0.76, respectively.
T. K. Ugur and A. Erdamar in [40] designed an algorithm based on a multistage method.
The ﬁrst stage included feature extraction, after obtaining the scalogram coefﬁcients.
The second stage included formation of test and training sets, in order to perform clas-
siﬁcation. The scalogram is calculated as the squared magnitude of the continuous
wavelet transform (CWT). The attenuation of arousals can be estimated, as the scale
shifts of the EEG signal in the scalogram, and indirectly by the scalogram mean. The
variance of the scalogram is related to arousal, due to the energy density increase. This
means, that both the mean and variance of the scalogram varies with arousal activity,
which were used as features. A support vector machine (SVM) classiﬁer was used,
based on ﬁve-fold cross validation, and the best training was obtained by a linear SVM
classiﬁer. The sensitivity, speciﬁcity and accuracy for the test set were 92.66%, 99.78%,
and 98%, respectively.
I. Fernández-Varela et. al. in [41] designed a multistage algorithm, where the ﬁrst
step included signal ﬁltering, with a high pass and notch ﬁlter, and artifact removal
by an adaptive ﬁlter. The second step detected arousal candidates, by searching for
abrupt frequency changes. This is done by comparing the power content, within a 3
second window, with the baseline levels from the past instants, which is calculated as the
28
==PAGE:59==
Chapter 3. State of the Art
3.4. SUMMARY
average power values. The third step, studied each candidate for recognizable arousal
patterns within EEG and EMG. The ﬁnal step removed false arousal detections, where
several methods were applied, all inspired by the clinical arousal deﬁnition. The method
had a sensitivity, speciﬁcity, and F1 score of 0.748, 0.988, and 0.793, respectively.

3.4
Summary
The studies investigating which features are signiﬁcantly for insomniacs, are summa-
rized in Table 3.2. The studies generally agree, that patients with insomnia have altered
sleep architecture. However, the studies do not agree on which features are signiﬁcantly
different between the two patient groups. Most studies, however, agree on a difference
in TST, WASO, and SE. According to the literature study, and to the best knowledge of
the author of this thesis, researchers have focused on investigation of group differences
throughout the entire sleep duration, and no studies have investigated group differences
in arousal speciﬁc features. An investigation of arousal speciﬁc features, combined with
features from the whole sleep duration will be conducted throughout this thesis.
For investigation of arousal speciﬁc features, an arousal detection method should be
applied to the data. Based on the literature study, most methods uses deep learning
techniques, and achieve good performances, however, most studies only use a small
dataset. The consequence of using a small data set, is that the proposed detector can be
highly affected by the used data, which results in an artiﬁcially increased performance
rate. Since the studies have used different performance scores, a direct comparison is
difﬁcult. All methods achieve a high performance, and a decision cannot be made based
on this. The decision is therefore based on the number of included participants, since
this will minimize the change of the results being artiﬁcially increased, thus the method
proposed by A. Brink-Kjaer et. al. will be implemented in this thesis.
