==PAGE:61==
CHAPTER 4
DATA ACQUISITION & AROUSAL DETECTION
This chapter provides a description of the different datasets, used for investigation of
insomnia related features. Furthermore, a thorough description of the arousal detector,
chosen based on the discussion in Section 3.3, is provided. Lastly, the preprocessing
steps of the datasets are explained, which include ﬁltering of the signals, extraction of
arousals, and how the datasets have been split into an insomnia and control group.
4.1
Data Acquisition
This thesis has used different datasets; the study of osteoporotic fractures in older men
(MrOS), Cleveland Family Study (CFS), and the project founded by the European Re-
search Council (ERCP) study. The MrOS and the CFS datasets are obtained from the
National Sleep Research Resource (NSRR) [42], [43], where the ERCP is acquired by
Eus J. W. Van Someren [44].
The NSRR is a system developed for sharing of sleep data, in order for researchers to
study the sleep disorders impact on different health outcomes. Currently, the NSRR
contains sleep data from more than 25,000 subjects and more than 30,000 signal ﬁles.
The National Institute of Health has funded several studies, including the MrOS and
CFS. The PSG recordings from these funded studies, were analyzed by a central Sleep
Reading Center [42].
4.1.1
MrOS Dataset
The study was designed to analyze the relationships between sleep disorders and vas-
cular disease, falls, fractures, and mortality in older men. From 2000 to 2002, 5,994 65
years and older community-dwelling men were located through voter and motor vehi-
cle registrations, as well as newspaper advertisements. The participants were enrolled
throughout the United States in six different clinical centers; Birmingham (Alabama),
Minneapolis (Minnesota), Palo Alto (California), the Monongahela Valley near Pitts-
burgh (Pennsylvania), Portland (Oregon), and San Diego (California). Inclusion criteria
31
==PAGE:62==
4.1. DATA ACQUISITION
Chapter 4. Data Acquisition & Arousal Detection
for the study were: 1) capability of walking without assistance, 2) no bilateral hip re-
placement, 3) capability of providing self-reported data, 4) staying near the clinical site
during the study, 5) absence of a medical condition, that would result in imminent death,
6) ability to understand and sign an informed consent, and 7) being at least 65 years of
age [42], [43], [45]–[47].
Between December 2003 and March 2005, 3,135 participants were chosen out of the
original participants, to engage in a comprehensive sleep assessment. Participants were
excluded if they used a mechanical device during sleep, such as a pressure mask for
sleep apnea, mouthpiece for snoring, or sleep apnea and oxygen therapy. The remaining
participants were not included due to, e.g. they refused, passed away before the sleep
visit, were not eligible, terminated the study, or was not asked since the recruitment
criterion of 3,000 was met. Of the included participants, 3,132 had available cogni-
tive function data, and out of these, 2,909 had an in-home overnight PSG recording
performed, but 39 had no available data on sleep architecture, resulting in 2,870 partic-
ipants remaining [42], [43], [45]–[47].
The in-home PSG recordings were completed using the unattended, portable PSG: the
Compumedics Saﬁro Sleep Monitoring System, which uses a high-pass pre-ﬁlter with
cut-off frequency at 0.16 Hz. The recordings were gathered within 1 month from the
clinic visit. The recording montage was: C3/A2 and C4/A1 EEGs, bilateral EOGs, a
bipolar submental EMG, thoracic and abdominal respiratory inductance plethysmogra-
phy, airﬂow by using a nasal–oral thermocouple and nasal pressure cannula, ﬁnger pulse
oximetry, lead I ECG, body position with mercury switch sensor, and bilateral tibialis
leg movements by piezoelectric sensors. The PSG recording was set up by trained and
certiﬁed staff, which veriﬁed the impedance values, conﬁrmed calibration of position
sensors, and noted any problems during the set up. The staff returned the next morning
to gather the equipment and download the data [42], [43], [45]–[47].
After the recordings had been collected, the sleep stages (REM and NREM 1-4, stage 3
and 4 were later combined) were scored, using the Rechtschaffen and Kales (RK) rules.
Different parameters were determined, e.g. complete airﬂow interruption for more than
10 seconds was deﬁned as an apnea event, where hypopneas were classiﬁed as more
than 10 seconds of clear depletion in breathing amplitude, more than 30% beneath the
baseline [42], [43], [45]–[47].
4.1.2
CFS Dataset
The study consisted of participants from 5 to 88 years of age, with focus on sleep ap-
nea. 2,284 participants were recruited from 361 families. All participants had in-home
examination by a trained technician, whom instructed the use of an overnight sleep mon-
32
==PAGE:63==
Chapter 4. Data Acquisition & Arousal Detection
4.1. DATA ACQUISITION
itor. For participants older than 13 years of age, medical history, medication use, family
history, and symptoms were assessed through questionnaires. A modiﬁed version was
given to the caretaker of the younger children. The overnight recording was performed
using a Compumedics E-series System, which uses a band-pass pre-ﬁlter with cut-off
frequencies at 0.16 Hz and 105 Hz. A recording of airﬂow, chest wall impedance, ﬁnger
pulse oximetry, and heart rate was performed as well. After the recording, it was scored
by certiﬁed technicians, with rules similar as in the MrOS study, which also applies to
the classiﬁcation of apneas and hypopneas [42], [43], [48], [49].
4.1.3
ERCP Dataset
This study focused on investigating the efﬁcacy of cognitive behavioral therapy for in-
somnia (CBT-I), chronobiological therapy, and their combination versus the usual treat-
ment. The 120 included participants were divided into four groups, the ﬁrst two to four
participants in each group were chosen at random, and the remaining were divided based
on matching variables, e.g. age, ISI, and insomnia subtype. The participants all met the
inclusion criteria: 1) fulﬁll the insomnia criteria of DSM-5 and ICSD-3, 2) ISI ≥10, 3)
classiﬁed as insomnia type 1, 2, or 3 according to the insomnia type questionnaire, and
4) age between 18 and 70 years. The exclusion criteria were: major depressive disorder,
antidepressant medication, current CBT-I treatment, other sleep disorder, neurological
or psychiatric disease, stroke, traumatic head injury, night work or rotating shift work,
eye condition incompatible with light exposure, light-induced migraine or epilepsy, and
MRI contra-indication. The participants were recruited through the Netherlands Sleep
Registry, media advertisements, and ﬂyers, and would have ﬁve visits:
• T0 at the beginning (week 0): nine days of actigraphy and sleep diaries, composite
international diagnostic interview, MRI, questionnaires, and division into the four
groups
• T1 at week 7: sevens days of actigraphy and sleep diaries, MRI, and question-
naires
• T2 and T3 at week 26 and 39, respectively: questionnaires
• T4 at week 52: 7 days actigraphy and sleep diaries, composite international diag-
nostic interview, and questionnaires
This study was part of a larger study, aimed at investigating predictors of developing
depression in people with insomnia. Therefore, another 30 participants were included,
with an insomnia subtype less prone to developing depression, and 30 age- and sex
matched controls. At T0 a nine day actigraphy and one night of high-density (256
channel) EEG recording was performed [44].
33
==PAGE:64==
4.1. DATA ACQUISITION
Chapter 4. Data Acquisition & Arousal Detection
Automatic Sleep Scoring
After the EEG recording had been conducted and collected, a sleep stage classiﬁcation
was performed by a real-time automatic classiﬁcation [50], see Figure 4.1.
The automatic sleep stage classiﬁcation had been trained and tested by a combination
of two datasets, comprised of PSG recordings from 1046 participants aged 15-19 years,
and from 284 participants aged 21-40 years. For each dataset, 75% was used for train-
ing, and 25% for testing. Afterwards, it was validated using another two datasets, com-
prised of PSG recordings from 210 participants aged 10-83 years, and from 77 partici-
pants aged 47-89 years [50].
Two EEG channels (C3-A2 and C4-A1) and two EOG channels (E1-A2 and E2-A1)
were used. The two EEG channels were averaged, resulting in one EEG channel. If one
EEG channel was missing, the channel was used without averaging. The three channels
were then ﬁltered using a window-based, with an order of 50, ﬁnite impulse response
(FIR)1 band-pass ﬁlter, with ﬁlter weights computed using a Hamming window. The
cut-off frequencies were 0.3 Hz and 45 Hz for the EEG channel, and 0.3 Hz and 12
Hz for the EOG channels. Afterwards, the channels were downsampled to 100 Hz us-
ing a polyphase FIR ﬁlter1. This step was skipped if the data already had a sampling
frequency of 100 Hz. The data were then divided into 30 second epochs, where a spec-
trogram was obtained using a Hamming window, with a length of 128 and an overlap of
29.69%, and
Figure 4.1: Overview of the automatic sleep stages classiﬁcation. The image has been
modiﬁed from [44].
1See Appendix D for a description of the concept.
34
==PAGE:65==
Chapter 4. Data Acquisition & Arousal Detection
4.2. AUTOMATIC AROUSAL DETECTION ALGORITHM
a Fast Fourier Transform algorithm. This resulted in a spectrogram for all three chan-
nels, with 32 time points and 65 frequency points, where only the ﬁrst 32 frequency
points were used. The spectrograms for each epoch were stacked into a matrix of size
32x32x3 [50].
The stacked spectrogram went through two stages of classiﬁcation. The ﬁrst stage con-
sisted of a 16-layers deep CNN1, which allocated class probabilities for each sleep stage
for the speciﬁc epoch. The ﬁnal deep CNN layer generated the ﬁve probabilities; pwake,
pstage1, pstage2, pstage3/4, and pREM, and the highest probability was the most probable
class (MPC). The ﬁve probabilities and MPCs were used as inputs to the second stage,
which consisted of a multi-layer perceptron1. The multi-layer perceptron weighted the
succeeding and preceding MPCs, and the current epochs probabilities and MPCs, in
order to adapt the class probabilities for each sleep stage. Finally, an MPC block was
used to ﬁnd the ﬁnal sleep stage for the given epoch, and its conﬁdence score [50].
The sleep stage classiﬁcation algorithm achieved a performance of 90% for the training
set, 89.9% for the test set, and 72.1-81.4% for the validation sets [50].
4.2
Automatic Arousal Detection Algorithm
The arousal detector, created by A. Brink-Kjaer et. al. [39], has been trained and tested
based on three different studies, including the MrOS, and the CFS. The PSG recording
in all datasets included at least a central (C3-A2) EEG derivation, left and right EOG,
chin EMG, and lead II ECG. Not all the included datasets had consistent microarousal
scoring, only MrOS and CFS had accurate and consistent scorings. Hence, the training
set based on 80% of randomly selected subjects from the MrOS and the CFS dataset, and
the remaining was used for testing, together with 271 recordings from a third dataset,
which has similar arousal scorings [39].
The detector uses a CNN to calculate descriptive signal features, by applying a series
of ﬁlters, see Figure 4.2. The designed ﬁlter kernels require an input with consistent
sampling frequencies, due to the static size, hence all signals were resampled to 128 Hz.
In order to avoid aliasing errors, an anti-aliasing low-pass least-squares FIR ﬁlter was
applied. For normalization of the ﬁlter gain, a Kaiser window2 with a shape factor β =
5 was used. For removal of power line interference (60 Hz) and low frequency artifacts,
an inﬁnite impulse response (IIR) ﬁlter was used. The IIR ﬁlter, using the Butterworth
method, was designed similar for EEG and EOG signals; pass-band frequencies from
0.5 Hz to 35 Hz, allowed pass-band ripple of 1 dB, stop-band frequencies at 0.1 Hz and
2See Appendix D for a description of the concept.
35
==PAGE:66==
4.2. AUTOMATIC AROUSAL DETECTION ALGORITHM
Chapter 4. Data Acquisition & Arousal Detection
Figure 4.2: Overview of the preprocessing steps in the arousal detector [39].
50 Hz, and a stop-band attenuation of 20 dB. For the EMG signals, the ﬁrst stop-band
ends a 5 Hz and the pass-band starts at 10 Hz, otherwise matching values. To avoid a
non-linear phase response and a frequency-dependent group delay, the ﬁlter was imple-
mented with zero-phase ﬁltering. For removal of ECG and ocular movement artifacts in
the EEG signal, a Recursive Least Square adaptive ﬁlter2, with a ﬁlter order of 4 and a
forgetting factor of 0.995, was implemented. Finally, the signals were standardized, by
subtracting the mean and dividing with the standard deviation (std) [39].
The CNN automatically described the preprocessed EEG, EOG, and EMG signals by
a feature set in 1 second bins. These features were then fed to a bi-directional Long
short-term memory (LSTM)2 network, followed by a fully connected neural network,
which predicted 2 sets of labels (wake vs sleep, and arousal vs non-arousal) as prob-
abilities. These probabilities were transformed into event detections, by some simple
postprocessing steps, see Table 4.1. An arousal event was detected, when the probabil-
ity exceeded 0.225, and was longer than 3 seconds [39].
Arousal
Wake
Threshold probability
P(Arousal) > 0.225
P(Wake) > 0.45
Connect events closer than
10 seconds
15 seconds
Remove events shorter than
3 seconds
15 seconds
Table 4.1: The postprocessing steps for the arousal detector when predicting events.

4.3. PREPROCESSING
4.3
Preprocessing
After ﬁltering, the EEG signal is divided into arousal sections. By using the detected
arousal position, obtained from the arousal detector, start- and endpoints for all arousals
are known. The arousal sections, based on EEG signals, are created by:
section(n) = EEGsignal(startpoint(n) : endpoint(n))
(4.1)
where n goes from 1 to the number of detected arousals.
Figure 4.3: Illustration of the C3-A2 EEG signal before ﬁltering, after ﬁltering, and
after EMG removal.
4.3.1
Group Division
The used datasets have been divided into an insomnia group and a control group. This
division has been made, based on the available parameters for each group, resulting in
slightly different division criteria. For the MrOS and the ERCP, the division has been
made based on the ISI value, where participants with an ISI value > 9 were allocated to
the insomnia group, and participants with ISI values ≤9 were allocated to the control
group. For the CFS, the division was made based on the physician-diagnosed insomnia
parameter, where participants with the diagnosis were allocated to the insomnia group,
and participants without the diagnosis were allocated to the control group. Participants
with missing information regarding the ISI value or the physician-diagnosed insomnia
parameter, were excluded from this thesis.

==PAGE:68==

CHAPTER 5
EXPLORATIVE RESEARCH ON INSOMNIA
This chapter explains the different insomnia related parameters, and how they are cal-
culated. First, a description of how an arousal is correlated with an apnea is explained.
Then, the calculation of different arousal related features are provided, both the fea-
tures that are valid for the entire sleep and those that are valid for speciﬁc sleep stages.
Finally, a description of the signiﬁcant test is provided, together with argumentation for
choice.
5.1
Respiratory Events
All datasets have besides sleep stage scoring, also scoring of respiratory events. Only
events regarding apneas are used. The scored apneas are divided into three types; cen-
tral apneas, obstructive apneas, and hypopneas. Central apneas occur when the proper
signals from the brain are not sent to the breathing controlling muscles, resulting in
repeated breathing stop and start during sleep. It is often a result of other conditions,
such as heart failure, strokes, and sleeping at high altitude [51]. Obstructive apneas
occur when the throat muscles relax and fall back to obstruct the airway, resulting in
repeated breathing stop and start during sleep [52]. Hypopnea is a mild version of ob-
structive sleep apnea, since hypopnea is only a 10 second period of reduced breathing
with ≥50% [53].
The startpoint for all apnea events is compared with all arousal startpoints. An apnea
induced arousal is classiﬁed if:
ApneaStart(k)−5·fs ≤startpoint(n) & ApneaStart(k)+10·fs ≥startpoint(n)
(5.1)
where k goes from 1 to number of apneas, and n goes from 1 to number of arousals. An
apnea induced arousal is classiﬁed if the arousal startpoint was placed between 5 sec-
onds before and 10 seconds after the apnea startpoint. This interval is chosen, since an
arousal awakes the subject, and hence stops the apnea event. This decision is supported
by Andreas Brink-Kjaer et. al. [39].
39
==PAGE:70==
5.2. AROUSAL RELATED FEATURES
Chapter 5. Explorative Research on Insomnia
The apnea induced arousal classiﬁcation is done for obstructive apneas, central apneas,
hypopneas, and for all apneas (the three types combined). How the features (amplitude,
power, duration, and frequency) for apnea induced arousals are calculated, is described
in Section 5.2.
5.2
Arousal Related Features
Based on Section 3.1, conventional sleep parameters, such as WASO and TST, would
be interesting to investigate, since most studies ﬁnd at least one signiﬁcant difference
within these parameters. Only a few studies have looked at sleep stage transitions, and
they found some signiﬁcant differences, and this could therefore be interesting to in-
vestigate as well. To the authors knowledge, no studies have investigated the arousal
appearance, and based on the theory in Section 2.4, this could be interesting to investi-
gate as well.
For the MrOS and the CFS study, WASO, SL (only MrOS), and SE are part of the avail-
able parameters for the datasets, and these values are extracted and used in this study.
It has not been possible to calculate the latter two features, since no lights off/on are
noted, and hence these features have not been calculated for the ERCP dataset.
Based on the available hypnogram, the TST can be calculated as:
TST = length(Hypnogram) −length(Hypnogram == Wake)
2/60
(5.2)
where length(Hypnogram) is the number of sleep stage epochs, and length(Hypnogram
== Wake) is the number of epochs classiﬁed as wake. The subtraction is divided by two,
and afterwards divided by 60, in order to gain the TST in hours. The TST is calculated
based on the number of epochs classiﬁed as a sleep stage. Based on the TST, the arousal
index can be calculated as:
ArousalIndex = length(ArousalSection)
TST
(5.3)
where length(ArousalSection) is the number of arousals.
For each arousal the arousal amplitude is calculated as:
amplitude(n) = max(ArousalSection(n)) −min(ArousalSection(n))
(5.4)
where n goes from 1 to the number of arousals.
40
==PAGE:71==
Chapter 5. Explorative Research on Insomnia
5.2. AROUSAL RELATED FEATURES
The arousal power for all arousals is calculated as:
power(n) = sum(ArousalSection(n)2)
length(ArousalSection(n)
(5.5)
The power is calculated as the sum of absolute squares, divided by the signal length.
Each arousal duration is calculated as:
duration(n) = length(ArousalSection(n))
fs
(5.6)
where fs is the sampling frequency.
The frequency for each arousal is calculated as:
peaksmax(n) = findpeaks(ArousalSection(n))
peaksmin(n) = findpeaks(−ArousalSection(n))
frequency(n) = length(peaksmax) + length(peaksmin)
2 · duration
(5.7)
where ﬁndpeaks1 is an inbuilt MATLAB function, that ﬁnds all peaks in a signal.
The TBA is calculated as:
TBA = startpoint(n + 1) −endpoint(n)
fs
(5.8)
where "endpoint(n)" is the endpoint for an arousal, and "startpoint(n+1)" is the startpoint
for the following arousal. Between two arousals, a period of wake can occur, in order to
exclude this awakening, the TBA is also calculated as:
WBA = length(find(Wake < startpoint(n + 1) & Wake > endpoint(n)))
TBAwithoutwake = startpoint(n + 1) −endpoint(n) −WBA
fs
(5.9)
where WBA is wake between arousals, calculated as the number of epochs classiﬁed as
wake between two arousals.
The number of arousals are calculated as:
ArousalNo = length(ArousalSection)
(5.10)
where length(ArousalSection) indicates the number of arousal detected by the arousal
detector.

5.2.1
Sleep Stages Related Features
Based on the hypnogram, it is possible to ﬁnd the number of shifts between each sleep
stage, this has been calculated as e.g.:
if (Hypnogram(h : h + 1) == [Wake N1])
then NoShiftsWN1 = NoShiftsWN1 + 1
(5.11)
where h goes from 1 to the number of epochs - 1. This is done for all possible sleep
stage transitions. The duration of each sleep stage is calculated as:
SSduration = length(SS)
fs/60/60
(5.12)
where length(SS) is the number of epochs classiﬁed as the speciﬁc sleep stage. The
number is divided by the sampling frequency, and twice divided by 60, in order to ob-
tain the duration in hours. This is done for all sleep stages.
Each arousal is separated into which sleep stage they started in, by:
if startpoint(n) == SS
then SSidx(idx) = n
(5.13)
where SS is the speciﬁc sleep stage, and idx is the number of arousals separated into the
speciﬁc sleep stage.
The arousal index for each sleep stage is calculated as:
ArousalIndexSS = length(ArousalSectionSS)
SSduration
(5.14)
where length(ArousalSectionSS) is the number of arousal, belonging to the speciﬁc
sleep stage.
Besides these, the arousal amplitude, power, duration, frequency, TBA, TBA without
wake, arousal number, and arousal index are also calculated for each arousal in the
speciﬁc sleep stage.
5.2.2
Test for Signiﬁcance
To test for signiﬁcant group differences for the different arousal features, a two-sample
t-test is performed, using the inbuilt MATLAB function ttest2. The two-sample t-test is
a parametric test, which compares two independent data samples, with the test statistics:
t =
¯x −¯y
q
s2x
n +
s2y
m
(5.15)
42
==PAGE:73==
Chapter 5. Explorative Research on Insomnia
5.2. AROUSAL RELATED FEATURES
where ¯x and ¯y are the sample means, s2
x and s2
y are the sample std, and n and m are
the sample sizes. The returned t value is a test decision for the null hypothesis; that
the two data samples are from independent, random samples with normal distribution,
equal means, and equal, unknown variances. The rejection or acceptance of the null
hypothesis is based on a 5% signiﬁcance level [54].
The decision of a parametric test is made, since a normal distribution is assumed in both
the insomnia and control group, and since a parametric test has more statistical power
[55]. A two-sample test is chosen, since it tests whether a sample is both higher or lower
than the other sample [56]. This is an important feature, since it is unknown whether
the insomnia group, will have a higher or lower feature value, compared to the control
group.

CHAPTER 6
OUTCOMES OF INSOMNIA RESEARCH
The chapter presents the results from the insomnia related feature research. First,
the results regarding the preprocessing of the datasets are provided. Next, the results
regarding the arousal speciﬁc features are presented, followed by the results regarding
the sleep stage transitions, and the apnea induced arousal features. Throughout the
chapter, it is described which features result in a signiﬁcant group difference.
6.1
Datasets Preprocessing
For all datasets, the automatic algorithm have been detecting arousals. In Figure 6.1, an
example of the arousal detection can be seen. The ﬁgure illustrates the eleventh record-
ing hours for one randomly chosen subject in the MrOS dataset. The EEG, EOG, EMG,
and ECG channels are illustrated, and the colored area is the arousal detection. Visually,
the algorithm has detected areas with an increased frequency in all channels, however,
this can not visually be seen on the ECG channel.
The group division for the three datasets, can be seen in Table 6.1-6.3. The Epworth
Sleepiness Scale (ESS) is a questionnaire, assessing the daytime sleepiness [57]. The
Pittsburg Sleep Quality Index (PSQI) score is a measurement of self-reported sleep qual-
ity and disturbances [58].
The MrOS dataset contains 151 insomnia patients and 865 controls, and the only signif-
icant group differences are on parameters related to insomnia; ESS, PSQI, and ISI. The
CFS dataset contains 35 insomniacs and 690 controls, and ﬁnds signiﬁcant differences
on age, and whether the participants have been diagnosed with insomnia, but not for the
other insomnia related parameters. The ERCP study has 158 participants with insomnia
and 47 controls, and only the ISI results in a signiﬁcant difference.

backup data, inferior/superior, or the jaw/cheek data are used. A third dataset is created,
as a combination of the primary and secondary scorings. By checking the class prob-
ability scores, the highest probability scores for each epoch is used independently, to
create the combined scorings.

6.2. AROUSAL SPECIFIC FEATURES
Chapter 6. Outcomes of Insomnia Research
6.2
Arousal Speciﬁc Features
Some features are calculated for each arousal, and hence one subject has multiple values,
where other features result in one value per subject. The features with only one value
are; arousal index, TST, WASO, SE, SL, number of sleep stage shifts, and number of
arousals. The features that has one value for each arousal are; amplitude, duration, fre-
quency, power, and TBA with and without including wake epochs. For these features,
an average, variance, and std value is calculated to make the signiﬁcance testing possi-
ble.
Figure 6.3a shows the TST values, for all subjects in the different datasets. It can be
seen, that insomnia and controls have approximately the same distributions, despite not
the same amount of subjects in each group. Based on this ﬁgure, no signiﬁcant group
difference is expected, however, by looking at Table 6.6, a signiﬁcant group difference
is found in the MrOS dataset.
Figure 6.3b shows the number of sleep stage transitions for all subjects in the three
datasets. Again, the same distribution tendency is seen for the insomnia and control
group, so no signiﬁcant group difference is expected, which is supported by the values
in Table 6.6.
For the duration, the average, variance, and std values are calculated, and these are il-
lustrated in Figure 6.4a. From this ﬁgure, it can be seen that the insomnia and control
group, for all duration values, follows the same distribution. A signiﬁcant group differ-
ence would not be expected, and in Table 6.6 no signiﬁcant difference is found, which
supports the histograms.
The TBA was calculated for all arousal, and had the average, variance and std values
calculated as well. Based on Figure 6.4b and Table 6.6, no signiﬁcant group differences
are detected, since the insomnia and control group follows the same distributions, for
all TBA values in the three datasets.
Table 6.6 shows a signiﬁcant group difference for WASO and SE in the MrOS dataset,
but not in the other datasets or for other features1. No other features result in a signiﬁ-
cant group difference for either datasets.

The features have been tested in multidimensional spaces, where two features are com-

is illustrated for all three datasets. The green dots are control subjects, and the blue dots
are insomnia subjects.
6.2.1
During Separate Sleep Stages
Some arousal features have been calculated for arousal in each sleep stage2, in order to
investigate if insomnia affects arousal in speciﬁc sleep stages. The arousal features have
been calculated in wake, N1, N2, N3, and REM sleep for all arousals belonging to the
2See Appendix C.2 for illustrations.
54
==PAGE:85==
Chapter 6. Outcomes of Insomnia Research
6.2. AROUSAL SPECIFIC FEATURES
speciﬁc sleep stage.
Figure 6.6 shows the number of arousal in each sleep stage for all three datasets. From
the ﬁgure, it can be seen that subjects in the MrOS dataset have more arousals in wake,
N1, and N2 sleep, compared to subjects in CFS and ERCP, but fewer arousal in N3
and REM sleep. It can also be seen, that the insomnia and control group have the same
distribution, regarding the number of arousal in all sleep stages and datasets, which sug-
gests that no group difference is present. In Table 6.7 it can be seen, that there are no
group differences for the number of arousals.
The average arousal amplitude is shown in Figure 6.7, which shows that both the in-
somnia and the control group follows the same distribution. Subjects in the ERCP study
have a higher arousal amplitude, compared to the subjects in the other studies. Based
on the ﬁgure, no group differences are expected, which is supported by Table 6.7.

==PAGE:88==
6.3. SLEEP STAGE TRANSITIONS FEATURES
Chapter 6. Outcomes of Insomnia Research
6.3
Sleep Stage Transitions Features
The sleep stage transitions have been investigated for each dataset, and illustrated in a
Markovian state diagram, see Figures 6.8-6.10 for the MrOS, CFS, and ERCP datasets,
respectively. Figure 6.8 shows that a signiﬁcant group difference for MrOS, is found
for sleep stage transitions N2 →W, N3→W, and N2 →REM, where the latter one has
a higher probability for controls. These signiﬁcant group differences are not found in
the other datasets. In the CFS dataset, a signiﬁcant group difference is found for wake
→REM, and from N3 →N3 sleep stage transitions, both with a higher probability for
controls, see Figure 6.9. For the ERCP dataset, only one signiﬁcant group difference is
found; N3 →N2, which has a higher probability for insomnia, see Figure 6.10.
In Figures 6.8-6.10, some transitions are not illustrated, due to less than half of the
subjects in both groups experiencing these transitions. These transitions are wake →
N3, N1 →N3, N3 →REM, REM →N3, and N3 →N1 for all datasets. The MrOS
and CFS also have less than half of the subjects experience N2 →N1, and REM →N2,
the latter one is only for the MrOS dataset. This indicates a higher agreement, between
which sleep stage transitions that do not occur, compared to those that occur more or
less in insomnia.

Figure 6.8: Markovian state diagram for the MrOS dataset. Red arrows indicate signiﬁ-
cantly higher transition probability for insomniacs; N1 →N3, N3 →wake, and N2 →
wake, and blue arrows indicate signiﬁcantly lower probability; N2 →REM. Gray ar-
rows indicates no signiﬁcant group difference. The following transitions, did not occur
in at least half of the participants in each group, and are therefore not visualized: wake
→N3, N1 →N3, N3 →REM, N3 →N1, REM →N2, REM →N3, and N2 →N1.

arousal, does not result in a signiﬁcant group difference for any dataset either, see Table

60
==PAGE:91==
Chapter 6. Outcomes of Insomnia Research
6.4. RESPIRATORY EVENTS FEATURES
shows in which sleep stages a signiﬁcant difference between apnea induced and non-
apnea induced occurs for insomnia, and Table 6.11 for controls. These tables show that
apnea has a large effect on the arousal appearance, through all sleep stages. Most of-
ten, the apneas decrease the arousal features, making the arousals less prominent. Only
the arousal duration, when looking at the entire sleep, is increased by apnea induced
arousal, or more speciﬁcally hypopneas.

trols, it would be expected to show a signiﬁcant group difference. Table 6.12 shows
where a signiﬁcant group difference is found for the arousal amplitude.
The table
shows that the group difference is signiﬁcant for almost all apnea and non-apnea in-
duced arousals in all sleep stages. Only the ERCP dataset experience situations with
no signiﬁcant group differences; often when looking at the sleep stage wake or N3, or
when looking at obstructive apnea induced arousals.

CHAPTER 7
EVALUATION OF INSOMNIA RESEARCH
This chapter discusses the results, obtained from the work done throughout this master
thesis. First, a discussion regarding the used datasets, the arousal detection, and the
included participants is provided. Then, the results are compared with the literature,
and the obtained differences are discussed. A comparison between the arousal and
insomnia deﬁnition, with the ﬁndings of this thesis, is discussed. Finally, a discussion
regarding the limitations of this thesis is provided.
7.1
The Analysed Datasets
The focus of the MrOS study was the relationship between sleep disorders and vascular
disease, falls, fractures, and mortality in older men, and the focus of the CFS study was
sleep apnea. Only the ERCP study had a focus on insomnia, but more speciﬁcally on the
insomnia treatment. Since the focus of the studies were different than the focus of this
thesis, the results could be affected. However, when choosing participants for the dif-
ferent studies, the subjects are chosen based on the inclusion criteria that ﬁt each study.
These criteria do not focus on the insomnia disease, and hence the subjects included
might not be the typical insomnia patients. If the subjects do not ﬁt the typical insom-
nia group, then the results might not be useful and applicable for research regarding
the insomnia disease. However, all studies have insomnia related information, so only
participants with insomnia related information is included in this thesis, which should
make the results applicable. The criteria of insomnia related parameters, excluded some
participants in the MrOS and the CFS dataset. By making sure the subjects have an ISI
higher than 9, or have been diagnosed with insomnia, the subjects should be a part of
the typical insomnia disease group, since they are expected to fulﬁl the criteria of the
insomnia deﬁnition. Hence, the results of this thesis, are not suspected to be inﬂuenced
or affected by the chosen studies population.
67
==PAGE:98==
7.1. THE ANALYSED DATASETS
Chapter 7. Evaluation of Insomnia Research
7.1.1
Automatic Scoring of Sleep Stages in the ERCP Dataset
The ERCP dataset had only 10 manual sleep stage scorings, and no respiratory events
detected, resulting in an automatic procedure being necessary. The used automatic sleep
staging algorithm has obtained good results in the testing and validation sets. However,
the ERCP dataset is new, unseen data, which the algorithm has not been validated on,
and therefore the classiﬁcation can not be trusted blindly.
When using the automatic sleep stage classiﬁcation, which EEG channels are used, is
decided by the user. Since there is no way of knowing which channels are sufﬁciently
clean for auto-scoring beforehand, two different sets of channels are used to generate a
primary and secondary set of scorings. The electrodes used for each scoring, are only 1
cm apart, meaning that the secondary scoring is based on the adjacent electrode pairs of
the primary scoring. A third set of scorings is created as well, based on a combination
of the primary and secondary scoring, which should result in a more robust scoring.
For deciding which scoring to use, it was compared against 10 manual scorings. The
agreement between the automatic and manual scorings were approximately 72% for the
combined scorings. This accuracy was deemed adequate for trusting the automatic sleep
stage scoring, since the validation set only consisted of 10 subjects.
The algorithm also detects the respiratory events, such as central apneas, obstructive ap-
neas, and hypopneas. The automatic algorithm uses not only EEG channels as input, but
also EMG, ECG, and chest and abdomen electrodes. During apneas the breathing either
stops or gets reduced, which could be detected through the chest and abdomen channels.
The event detection is assumed to be correct, even though it can not be validated on this
dataset, but since the detection is based on multiple electrodes, it is deemed adequate
for future analysis.
7.1.2
Automatic Arousal Detection Algorithm
The automatic arousal detection is validated on the MrOS and the CFS dataset with
a high performance, and the detection is therefore assumed to be correct within these
datasets. The detection algorithm has not been validated on the ERCP dataset, and can
not be done, since no manual arousal annotations exists. Arousal is deﬁned based on
activity in the EEG signal and muscle activity in the EMG signal (the latter one is only
in REM sleep). Since the detection algorithm uses both EEG and EMG channels, and
achieve a high accuracy when validated on a large set of subjects, the arousal detection
is assumed to be robust enough to detect arousals, with a high accuracy in an unseen
dataset, such as the ERCP.
68
==PAGE:99==
Chapter 7. Evaluation of Insomnia Research
7.2. FEATURE COMPARISON WITH THE LITERATURE
7.1.3
Included Participants
From the MrOS study, 1,020 participants are included, from the CFS study, 730 par-
ticipants are included, and from the ERCP study, 205 participants are included in this
thesis, giving a total of 1,955 participants. This is, from a technical view, believed to be
enough to express the diversity between the insomnia and the control group.
Given that insomnia has multiple subtypes, it will not be possible to comment on the
meaning of the ﬁndings in all insomnia subtypes. However, the ERCP only consists of
subjects with PI, and the results can be used to comment on the ﬁndings relation to PI.
PI is often seen in younger women and rarely seen in elderly, hence the MrOS is not
expected to have any PI subjects included. Often, the insomnia seen in elderly is due to
other external factors, such as medicine and other diseases. The insomnia group in the
MrOS, is therefore expected to be insomnia due to comorbidities. The CFS has subjects
in all ages, so determining which insomnia type the subjects have, is not possible. It
is most likely a combination of PI, insomnia due to external factors and comorbidities,
and other insomnia subtypes.
7.1.4
Group Division
A group division based on insomnia related parameters is made. For the MrOS and
ERCP datasets, a group division is made based on the ISI value, and for the CFS it
is made based on a clinical diagnosis of insomnia. The group division of the CFS
study is expected to be fairly representative of the insomnia and control populations.
The group division of the MrOS and ERCP studies are only based on the ISI values,
which is, independently, not enough for a diagnosis. However, the ISI scores give an
insight into the sleep difﬁculty, and often an ISI value of 9 indicates mild insomnia with
increasing severity. When looking at Table 6.1, a signiﬁcant group difference is seen
for all insomnia related parameters, and the group division based on the ISI values, is
therefore believed to result in a good representation of the groups.
7.2
Feature Comparison with the Literature
Based on the studies discussed in Section 3.1, and from the theory described in Sec-
tion 2.4, it is expected to ﬁnd signiﬁcant group differences for the conventional sleep
parameters, such as TST, SE, WASO, SOL, and sleep stage durations, as well as for the
arousal number, arousal index, and the TBA. This is not found during this thesis, and
will be discussed below.
Other researchers have studied conventional sleep parameters, when investigating group
69
==PAGE:100==
7.2. FEATURE COMPARISON WITH THE LITERATURE
Chapter 7. Evaluation of Insomnia Research
differences. These studies do not agree on which parameters that are signiﬁcantly differ-
ent, between insomnia and controls. Most studies agree on a difference in TST, WASO,
and SE. This is supported by the ﬁndings in the MrOS dataset, but not in the CFS or
ERCP datasets, see Table 6.6. When looking at the different study populations in the
MrOS and CFS, the ﬁndings could suggest, that the conventional sleep parameters are
affected by insomnia subtypes related to medicine or comorbidities. Thus, that the sleep
architecture is not affected by the insomnia disease, but by other disorders and medica-
tion. This is also supported by the ﬁndings in the ERCP, since no group difference is
found for the available conventional sleep parameters.
Table 6.6 also shows the arousal speciﬁc feature during the entire sleep. These features
do not result in any signiﬁcant group differences, indicating that the insomnia disease
does not affect the overall arousal appearance. The same applies for the number of sleep
stage transitions, the number of arousals, and the TBA. The number of arousals was ex-
pected to result in a signiﬁcant group difference, since the insomnia patients experience
getting less sleep during the night. This can not be supported by the ﬁndings, which
could suggest that patients with insomnia are more attentive to their sleep, which could
give the impression of being awake.
The number of arousals, sleep stage durations, and arousal features for speciﬁc sleep
stages, did not result in any signiﬁcant group differences for all three datasets, see Table
6.7. No similar signiﬁcant group difference is found between any two datasets. This
could support the suggestion, that the insomnia disease does not affect the number of
arousals, their appearance, or the time spent in each sleep stage.
7.2.1
Sleep Stage Transitions Differences
Based on the article by Yishul Wei et. al. [22], it is expected to ﬁnd an increased transi-
tions probability for insomniacs between sleep stage N2 to stage N1 and to wake. This
is not found throughout this thesis, with the exception of the transitions from stage N2
to wake in the MrOS dataset. From the Markovian state diagrams in Figure 6.8-6.10,
no signiﬁcant group difference is found in more than one dataset. When looking at the
MrOS dataset, a signiﬁcantly increased transition probability for insomniacs is found
from stage N2 to wake, and from N3 to wake. This could indicate, that the insomnia
subjects are more likely to wake up during sleep. A signiﬁcantly increased transition
probability for controls is found from stage N2 to REM sleep, which could indicate that
the controls are more likely to go into deeper sleep faster than insomniacs.
For the CFS study, no signiﬁcantly increased probabilities for insomniacs are found,
which could indicate, that the before mentioned difference, is due to comorbidities, in-
stead of the insomnia disease. However, a signiﬁcantly increased transition from wake
70
==PAGE:101==
Chapter 7. Evaluation of Insomnia Research
7.2. FEATURE COMPARISON WITH THE LITERATURE
to REM sleep is found for controls, which is the hallmark of narcolepsy [59]. Why this
is seen in controls is unclear.
When looking at the ERCP study, only one signiﬁcant difference is found; an increased
probability for insomniacs to shift from stage N3 to N2. This could support the ﬁndings
in the MrOS dataset; that insomniacs are less likely to experience sustained deep sleep
in comparison to controls.
7.2.2
Apnea Induced Arousal Feature Differences
The apnea induced arousals have been deﬁned based on the arousal and apnea start-
points. However, since it is expected that the apnea stops the arousals, it might provide
better results if looking at the arousal endpoint and apnea startpoint. However, it is
expected to give the approximately same results, and the ﬁndings are therefore still ex-
pected to be valid.
The number or arousal index of apnea induced arousals, whether it is central apnea, ob-
structive apnea, hypopneas, or all types of apneas, does not result in a signiﬁcant group
difference in any of the investigated datasets. This indicates that insomniacs and con-
trols experience the same amount of apnea induced arousals.
However, when looking into the appearance of the apnea and non-apnea induced arousals,
a signiﬁcant group difference is found for all arousal features and in all sleep stages for
the MrOS and CFS datasets. But, when looking at the ERCP, a signiﬁcant group differ-
ence is present most of the time for the arousal duration and frequency. For the arousal
amplitude, the signiﬁcant difference is present for most arousals in stage N1, N2, REM,
and through the entire sleep, and only for a few arousal types in wake and N3 sleep.
The arousal power is in two thirds of the cases not signiﬁcantly different between the
groups. This could indicate that the height of an apnea induced arousal in PI is less
affected, compared to the arousal frequency and duration.
These ﬁndings could suggest, that non-apnea and apnea induced arousals are highly
affected by the insomnia disease, especially the frequency and duration, where the am-
plitude and power is more affected by the reasons for the insomnia disease, such as
medicine or other diseases.
71
==PAGE:102==
7.3. REFLECTION ON THE AROUSAL DEFINITION
Chapter 7. Evaluation of Insomnia Research
7.3
Reﬂection on the Arousal Deﬁnition
For arousal scoring in NREM sleep, only EEG channels are needed, but when scored in
REM sleep, EMG channels are needed as well. Why should the motor activity only be
considered in REM sleep, and not in NREM sleep? In NREM sleep, an arousal event
can be detected with ease, due to the NREM activity appearance, but in REM sleep,
arousals are hard to detect, due to higher frequencies resembling wakefulness.
The deﬁnition of arousals is deﬁned in the AASM manual, which was established in
1975 [60], and the manual was based on existing knowledge, hence the knowledge re-
garding arousals are discovered before. The knowledge and computational resources
have improved, which drives the needs for a more consistent and updated arousal def-
inition. The criteria, regarding that the arousals need to proceed 10 seconds of stable
sleep, and that motor activity should be included in detection during REM sleep, results
in arousals being detected in NREM sleep, not to be considered as arousals in REM
sleep. Today, an arousal detection can be performed more consistently if using an au-
tomatic detector. The detector can detect very small changes in the frequencies, and if
the detector uses its own deﬁnition of frequency shifts in the EEG, and not mimicking
manual annotations, the needs for including EMG channels are not necessary.
If the deﬁnition of arousals are outdated, this will inﬂuence the hyperarousal theory
regarding insomnia. This will be discussed in the next section.
7.4
Reﬂection on the Insomnia Deﬁnition
The insomnia deﬁnition are made, when at least one of the listed problems are present:
• Hard time initiating sleep
• Struggle to maintain sleep, and waking up frequently during the night
• Wake up too early and being unable to go back to sleep
• The sleep is nonrestorative, or of poor quality
This deﬁnition is highly heterogenic, meaning that there could be multiple causes ex-
plaining each problem. A hard time to initiate sleep, could be due to e.g. too much light,
loud noises, worrying, pain, and waking up too early could be due to loud noises, the
bed partner, and/or the temperature etc. Due to the many reasons for each problem, the
deﬁnition is very imprecise, making it hard to ﬁnd speciﬁc elements during the sleep,
that are signiﬁcantly different for insomniacs.
72
==PAGE:103==
Chapter 7. Evaluation of Insomnia Research
7.5. LIMITATIONS
The hyperarousal theory, concerning insomnia, suggests an increased arousability, though,
this can not be veriﬁed in this thesis, despite a large amount of subjects included. The
ﬁndings of this thesis, suggest that arousals and sleep stage transitions do not cause
insomnia. For subjects with PI, the ERCP dataset, no clear, signiﬁcant differences are
found, which questions the hypothesis. This thesis ﬁnds large group differences, when
looking at apnea induced arousals, which indicates that what drives the occurrence of
arousals, is other factors such as sleep apnea. No clear group difference is found for
sleep stage transitions, and no markers that drives the hypothesis underlying insomnia,
are found.
7.5
Limitations
When performing e.g. 100 separate tests with a signiﬁcant level of 0.05, it is expected
to result in ﬁve tests deemed signiﬁcant by chance, called type I error. The family-
wise error rate (FWER) describes the probability of at least one type I error, and can be
calculated as:
FWER = 1 −(1 −α)m
(7.1)
where FWER is the family-wise error rate, alpha is the signiﬁcant level, and m is the
number of tests performed [61]. Throughout this thesis, approximately 400 signiﬁcant
tests have been performed for each dataset, resulting in a FWER > 0.99. This indi-
cates that there is an ∼100% chance, that at least one type I error has occured. This
indicates, that using a two-sample t-test, might not be an optimal choice, and should be
replaced by another, more accurate, test.
The FWER can be controlled using several methods, e.g. the Bonferroni method. This
method ensures the overall signiﬁcant level, by decreasing the signiﬁcant level for each
test to α/m [62]. In the case of this thesis, the signiﬁcant level for each test would then
be αbonferroni = 0.05/400 = 1.25 · 10−4. Using this signiﬁcant level, fewer signiﬁcant
group differences would be observed, e.g. no signiﬁcant group difference for TST,
WASO, and no arousal speciﬁc features in any sleep stages would be found.
73
==PAGE:104==
==PAGE:105==
CHAPTER 8
CONCLUSION
The purpose of this thesis, was to investigate speciﬁc physiological features for insom-
nia, in order to gain more knowledge and insight into the disease. To ensure that the
thesis provided new information to the research area, it was initiated with a compre-
hensive literature study regarding state of the art work. This was used in the decision
regarding which features that should be investigated.
The conventional sleep parameters were checked for signiﬁcant group differences be-
tween insomnia and controls, but no clear pattern was seen for insomniacs. The same
applies to features regarding the sleep stages and their transitions. However, signif-
icant differences were found for the arousal appearance, when investigating arousals
induced by different apnea types. These ﬁndings suggest, that insomnia patients do not
have increased arousability, and the hyperarousal theory might not be correct or precise
enough for the current insomnia deﬁnition. The ﬁndings suggest, that insomnia is driven
by other factors, such as apnea.
This thesis therefore challenges the deﬁnition of the hyperarousal theory, since no clear
correlation between insomniacs and arousal speciﬁc features, sleep stage transitions,
and conventional sleep parameters was found.
75
==PAGE:106==
==PAGE:107==
CHAPTER 9
FUTURE PERSPECTIVES
For future work, datasets with focus on the insomnia disease, and its subtypes, should be
used for analysis. This would provide more explanation regarding the different types of
insomnia, and whether the ﬁndings and suggestions within this thesis can be supported.
The arousal features should be investigated for signiﬁcant differences between insomnia
and controls, in order to investigate whether arousal differences are due to the insomnia
disease or other factors. By doing this, information regarding the validity of the hyper-
arousal theory, and if the insomnia deﬁnition is concrete enough, will be provided, as
well as whether or not the challenges this thesis presents, can be supported.
During future studies, other methods for testing the group signiﬁcance should be used.
The signiﬁcance should be determined, based on methods that corrects the family-wise
error rate, e.g. Bonferroni correction. This would ensure correct detection of signiﬁcant
group differences, and hence the results would be more valid, when discussing the in-
terpretation of the results.
More robust methods could be used for determining group differences based on arousal
features, e.g. a signiﬁcant arousal feature combination for the insomnia disease, could
be determined based on cross validation or forwards/backwards selection. Other meth-
ods for detecting arousal features could be used as well, e.g. deep learning methods, that
ﬁnds features which separate the two groups. These features might not be the traditional
parameters, but they could provide useful information regarding the disease.

SLEEPJ, 2020, 1–13

Manuscript in Preparation
Original Article
O R I G I N A L A R T I C L E
Challenge of the Insomnia Definition and the
Hyperarousal Theory
Rebecca Borum Rusborg1,*
1Department of Health Technology, Technical University of Denmark, Kongens Lyngby, Denmark
*Corresponding author at: Technical University of Denmark, Department of Health Technology, Ørsteds Plads, Building 345c, 2800 Kgs. Lyngby
Denmark.
E-mail address: rboru@dtu.dk
Abstract
Project description: Adequate sleep is central for health and normal cognitive functioning. Poor sleep quality causes
societal burden: insomnia, which is the most common sleep disorder. However, the basic understanding of the
sleep-wake processor is inadequate; an unmet need is the understanding of the relation of presence of macro-sleep
despite the perception of poor, light, or inadequate sleep. Therefore, the study aims to use an automatically annotated
dataset for analysis of arousal morphology, to discover physiological features that are specific for subjects with insomnia.
Methodology: All three datasets had arousals automatically detected by a validated detector, and were preprocessed
afterwards. The detected arousals were investigated through different features, e.g. amplitude, duration, and frequency.
These were calculated throughout the entire sleep and in each sleep stage. The arousals were also related to apnea events,
to see if the arousals got affected by apneas, and if affected differently in subjects with insomnia and controls.
Conventional sleep parameters, such as sleep latency, wake after sleep onset etc., and features regarding the sleep stage
transitions, were also tested for significant group differences.
Results: Only a few significant group differences were found, and non were consistently within all three datasets. The
significant group differences included: total sleep time, wake after sleep onset, sleep efficiency, arousal duration in wake,
variance of arousal power in N1, and sleep stage transitions between N2 and wake, wake and REM, as well as N3 and N2.
The apnea induced arousal features resulted in significant group differences, throughout all sleep stages in two datasets,
with a few exceptions. The last dataset had only significant group differences in approximately half of the cases.
Conclusion: The typical findings and theory regarding insomnia are challenged by the findings of this study, since no
consistent group difference was found. However, researching the apnea induced arousals might provide useful
information, for deeper understanding of the insomnia characteristics and classification.
Statement of Significance
The insomnia diagnosis is based purely on complaints from the patients, regarding poor sleep quality, unable to fall asleep, and
waking up too early. This could be due to multiple explanations, that has nothing to do with the patient having a sleep dis-
order. To only diagnose patients with sleep disorders, some objective sleep parameters specific for the insomnia disease are needed.
Key words: Insomnia; features; morphology; group differences; hyperarousal theory; challenge; EEG analysis; sleep
Submitted: XX XXX, 2021; Revised: XX XXX, 2021

==PAGE:117==

SLEEPJ, 2020, Vol. 00, No. 0
Introduction
Good sleep is central for health and normal cognitive function-
ing [1], since poor sleep or sleep loss will have a negative ef-
fect on a person’s well-being. Studies have shown that a short
sleep duration and correlated sleep disorders, e.g. insomnia,
will result in lower happiness. Sequelae of chronic sleep loss
includes diabetes, cardiovascular diseases, and reduced capac-
ity of managing daily tasks [2]. For diagnosing sleep disorders,
different procedures can be used, but often a polysomnographic
(PSG) test is conducted [1, 3, 4]. However, it is not required to
perform a PSG test to diagnose e.g. insomnia, unless disorders
such as sleep apnea, are suspected [3].
Present, the insomnia diagnosis is based on subjective sleep
questionnaires assessing the sleep behavior, and on sleep di-
aries, and hence is based on complaints from the patient re-
garding prolonged sleep latency, difficultly maintaining sleep,
and experience of non-refreshing or poor sleep in combination
with impaired daytime functioning. [5, 6, 3]. The diagnostic
criteria does not include objective sleep quality, due to e.g. lack
of consistent validated defining sleep parameters [5]. Based on
these diagnostic criteria, the insomnia prevalence is up to 50-
60%, depending on the population and criteria applied [6, 7, 8].
The insomnia disorder can be classified into different subtypes,
depending on the used manual. The Diagnostic and Statisti-
cal Manual of Mental Disorders - 5th Edition [? ] (DSM-5)
has three subtypes; primary insomnia (PI), insomnia related
to medical or mental disease, and insomnia related to the in-
take or abuse of substances. The International Classification
of Sleep Disorders - 3rd Edition [? ] (ICSD-3) has 11 subtypes,
including acute insomnia, psychophysiological insomnia, para-
doxical insomnia, idiopathic insomnia, and substance-induced
insomnia [6, 3].
In PSG studies of subjects with insomnia compared to
healthy subjects, small differences have been observed. These
differences included decreased total sleep time (TST) by 30-
60 minutes, slightly increased sleep onset latency (SOL), de-
creased sleep efficiency, increased amount of nocturnal awak-
enings, and increased wakefulness after sleep onset (WASO)
[9, 5, 10, 11, 12, 13, 14, 15]. These findings are more pronounced
in subjective assessments, indicating that a deeper understand-
ing of the discrepancy between objective and subjective sleep
parameters is essential [5, 6].
It is proposed that this dis-
crepancy can be explained by patients misperceiving sleep as
wake, they have increased attention on sleep-related threats,
and they have increased amounts of short awakenings during
the night, called arousals [5].
It is unlikely that insomnia is caused by a single gene, but
more likely several genes. This results in a physiological pre-
disposed risk or vulnerability of insomnia, which will be trig-
gered under environmental circumstances, such as stress and
conflicts. Studies have found that the insomnia onset was re-
lated to stress or life changes [6, 3]. A familial association with
light, disrupted sleep within patients with insomnia has been
reported as well, and an anxious, overconcerned behavior re-
garding health could be a predisposing factor, indicating that
the subjects appear prone to worrying [3].
The pathophysiology of insomnia is based on an interaction
between psychological and physiological factors. The hyper-
arousal theory is therefor an integrative approach to the patho-
physiology. It suggests that patients who ruminate about their
sleep complaints are susceptible to developing "learned sleep
preventing associations", which explains the chronicity of the
insomnia disorder. Other behaviors are also suggested to af-
fect the presence of insomnia, such as daytime napping and
increased alcohol consumption.
Acute insomnia is triggered
by different stressors, and often the sleep complaints resolve
when removing the stressors again. However, some develop
chronic insomnia which is independent of the stressors [6].
A perpetuating factor in chronic insomnia can be arousals,
which can be expressed in terms of somatic, cognitive or corti-
cal activation. Cortical arousal manifests as increased frequen-
cies in the EEG, resultsing in irregular levels of sensory and
information processing, as well as long-term memory forma-
tion. These arousals are associated with sleep continuity dis-
turbances and/or sleep state misperception. Increased sensory
processing results in increased vulnerability to environmental
stimuli, which affects the sleep initiation and/or maintenance.
The increased information processing affects the distinction
between sleep and wakefulness, resulting in the perception of
sleep as wake. These increased factors are expected to corre-
late with altered SL, WASO and sleep duration [6].
The cortical arousals, dubbed arousals throughout this pa-
per, can be detected in EEG signals, and are defined as an
abrupt shift in EEG frequency for more than 3 seconds with
a minimum 10 seconds of previously stable sleep. During the
sleep stage Rapid Eye Movement (REM) sleep, arousals also
require an increase in the submental EMG for at least 1 sec-
ond. Patients with insomnia experienced an increased amount
of arousals, resulting in a fragmented sleep process, which pre-
vents restful sleep and results in daytime sleepiness [1, 4].
The fragmented sleep seen in patients with insomnia, and
the poor sleep and sleep loss, results in an overall poor life
quality with depression and increased accidents [8, 16]. These
symptoms and the high prevalence, drive researchers to gain
more knowledge about the disease and its possible treatment
methods.
The understanding of the insomnia pathophysiol-
ogy is difficult, due to the not fully understood complex psy-
choneurobiology. Recently, hyperarousal and stress dysregula-
tion models of insomnia, have provided essential insight into
the insomnia disease process [17].
The purpose of this study is therefore to investigate differ-
ent features, both arousal specific and more general sleep fea-
tures, in order to gain more insight into the disease process.
Methods
Data Acquisition
Diversity is important to ensure valid results, and the suf-
ficient data diversity was ensured by using data from three
different cohorts, scored by different sleep technicians, based
at various sleep centers, or by an automatic algorithm. The
included cohorts are the MrOS Sleep Study, the Cleveland
Family Study (CFS), and the ERCP study. The MrOS and the
CFS datasets are obtained from the National Sleep Research
Resource (NSRR) [18, 19], where the ERCP are acquired by Eus
J. W. Van Someren [20].
MrOS Dataset
The study was designed to analyze the relationships between
sleep disorders and vascular disease, falls, fractures, and mor-
tality in older men.
5,994 65 years and older community-
dwelling men were recruited and enrolled throughout the
United States in six different clinical centers; Birmingham (Al-
abama), Minneapolis (Minnesota), Palo Alto (California), the
Monongahela Valley near Pittsburgh (Pennsylvania), Portland
(Oregon), and San Diego (California).
Inclusion criteria for
the study were: 1) capability of walking without assistance,
2) no bilateral hip replacement, 3) capability of providing self-
reported data, 4) staying near the clinical site during the study,
5) absence of a medical condition, that would result in immi-
nent death, 6) ability to understand and sign an informed con-
sent, and 7) being at least 65 years of age [21, 22, 23, 19, 18].
==PAGE:118==
Rebecca B. Rusborg et al.
|
3
Between December 2003 and March 2005, 2,870 partici-
pants were chosen out of the original participants, to engage
in a comprehensive sleep assessment, and had an in-home
overnight PSG recording performed.
Participants were ex-
cluded if they used a mechanical device during sleep, such as
a pressure mask for sleep apnea, mouthpiece for snoring or
sleep apnea and oxygen therapy.
The in-home PSG record-
ings were completed using the unattended, portable PSG: the
Compumedics Safiro Sleep Monitoring System, which uses a
high-pass pre-filter with cut-off frequency at 0.16 Hz. After
the recordings had been collected, the sleep stages (REM and
NREM 1-4, stage 3 and 4 were later combined) were scored,
using the Rechtschaffen and Kales (RK) rules.
Different pa-
rameters were determined, e.g. complete airflow interruption
for more than 10 seconds was defined as an apnea event, where
hypopneas were classified as more than 10 seconds of clear de-
pletion in breathing amplitude, more than 30% beneath the
baseline [21, 22, 23, 19, 18].
Cleveland Family Study Dataset
The study consisted of participants from 5 to 88 years of age,
with focus on sleep apnea. 2,284 participants were recruited
from 361 families.
All participants had in-home examina-
tion by a trained technician, whom instructed the use of an
overnight sleep monitor. For participants older than 13 years
of age, medical history, medication use, family history, and
symptoms were assessed through questionnaires. A modified
version was given to the caretaker of the younger children. The
overnight recording was performed using a Compumedics E-
series System, which uses a band-pass pre-filter with cut-off
frequencies at 0.16 Hz and 105 Hz.
A recording of airflow,
chest wall impedance, finger pulse oximetry, and heart rate
was performed as well. After the recording, it was scored by
certified technicians, with rules similar as in the MrOS study,
which also applies to the classification of apneas and hypop-
neas [24, 25, 19, 18].
ERCP Dataset
This study focused on investigating the efficacy of different
treatments for insomnia. 120 participants were included, and
they all met the inclusion criteria: 1) fulfill the insomnia crite-
ria of DSM-5 and ICSD-3, 2) ISI ≥10, 3) classified as insomnia
type 1, 2, or 3 according to the insomnia type questionnaire,
and 4) age between 18 and 70 years.
The exclusion criteria
were: major depressive disorder, antidepressant medication,
current CBT-I treatment, other sleep disorder, neurological or
psychiatric disease, stroke, traumatic head injury, night work
or rotating shift work, eye condition incompatible with light
exposure, light-induced migraine or epilepsy, and MRI contra-
indication. This study was part of a larger study, aimed at in-
vestigating predictors of developing depression in people with
insomnia. Therefore, another 30 participants were included,
with an insomnia subtype less prone to developing depression,
and 30 age- and sex matched controls. At T0 a nine day actigra-
phy and one night of high-density (256 channel) EEG recording
was performed [20].
After the EEG recording had been conducted and collected,
a sleep stage classification was performed by a real-time au-
tomatic classification [26]. Two EEG channels (C3-A2 and C4-
A1) and two EOG channels (E1-A2 and E2-A1) were used. The
two EEG channels were averaged, resulting in one EEG chan-
nel. The three channels were then filtered using a window-
based finite impulse response (FIR) band-pass filter, and after-
wards downsampled to 100 Hz. The data were then divided into
30 second epochs, where a spectrogram was obtained using
a Hamming window. The stacked spectrogram went through
two stages of classification. The first stage consisted of a 16-
layers deep CNN, which allocated class probabilities for each
sleep stage for the specific epoch. The final deep CNN layer gen-
erated the five probabilities; pwake, pstage1, pstage2, pstage3/4, and
pREM, and the highest probability was the most probable class
(MPC). The five probabilities and MPCs were used as inputs to
the second stage, which consisted of a multi-layer perceptron
(MLP). The MLP weighted the succeeding and preceding MPCs,
in order to adapt the class probabilities for each sleep stage. Fi-
nally, an MPC block was used to find the final sleep stage for
the given epoch, and its confidence score [26].
Automatic Arousal Detection Algorithm
All datasets had arousals detected by the arousal detector cre-
ated by Andreas Brink-Kjaer et. al. [27]. The detector has been
trained and tested based on three different study datasets; the
MrOS, the CFS, and the Wisconsin Sleep Cohort dataset.
The detector uses a CNN to calculate descriptive signal fea-
tures, by applying a series of filters. The designed filter kernels
require an input with consistent sampling frequencies, due to
the static size, hence all signals were resampled to 128 Hz. In
order to avoid aliasing errors, an anti-aliasing low-pass least-
squares FIR filter was applied. For removal of power line inter-
ference (60 Hz) and low frequency artifacts, an infinite impulse
response (IIR) filter was used. To avoid a non-linear phase re-
sponse and a frequency-dependent group delay, the filter was
implemented with zero-phase filtering. For removal of ECG
and ocular movement artifacts in the EEG signal, a Recursive
Least Square (RLS) adaptive filter was implemented. Finally,
the signals were standardized, by subtracting the mean and
dividing with the standard deviation (SD) [27].
The CNN automatically describes the preprocessed EEG,
EOG, and EMG signals by a feature set in 1 second bins. These
features are then fed to a bi-directional Long short-term mem-
ory (LSTM) network, followed by a fully connected neural net-
work, which predicts arousals vs non-arousals as probabili-
ties. An arousal event is detected, when the probability exceeds
0.225, and are longer than 3 seconds, where events closer than
10 seconds are combined [27].
Preprocessing
The EEG signal is filtered, using the same IIR filter using the
Butterworth method, with pass-band frequencies from 0.5 Hz
to 35 Hz, allowed pass-band ripple of 1 dB, stop-band frequen-
cies at 0.1 Hz and 50 Hz, and stop-band attenuation of 20 dB.
After the EEG signal is filtered, the ECG artifacts needed to be
removed, but first the ECG signal is resampled, to the same
sampling frequency as the EEG signal.
For ECG artifact re-
moval, a Recursive Least Square (RLS) adaptive filter, with a
filter order of 4 and a forgetting factor of 0.995, was imple-
mented. After filtering, the EEG signal is divided into arousal
sections. By using the detected arousal position, obtained from
the arousal detector, start- and endpoints for all arousals are
known. The arousal sections, based on EEG signals, are created
by:
section(n) = EEGsignal(startpoint(n) : endpoint(n))
(1)
where n goes from 1 to the number of arousals detected.
The used datasets have been divided into an insomnia
group and a control group.
This division has been made,
based on the available parameters for each group, resulting
in slightly different division criteria.
For the MrOS and the
ERCP, the division has been made based on the ISI value,
where participants with an ISI value > 9 were allocated to the
insomnia group, and participants with ISI values ≤9 were
allocated to the control group. For the CFS, the division was
==PAGE:119==
4
|
SLEEPJ, 2020, Vol. 00, No. 0
made based on the physician-diagnosed insomnia parameter,
where
participants
with
the
diagnosis
were
allocated
to
the insomnia group, and participants without the diagno-
sis were allocated to the control group.
Participants with
missing information regarding the ISI value or the physician-
diagnosed insomnia parameter, were excluded from this study.
Respiratory Events
All datasets have besides sleep stage scoring, also scoring of
apneas. The scored apneas are divided into three types; central
apneas, obstructive apneas, and hypopneas. The startpoint for
all apnea events is compared with all arousal startpoints. An
apnea induced arousal is classified if:
ApneaStart(k) – 5 · fs ≤startpoint(n)
& ApneaStart(k) + 10 · fs ≥startpoint(n)
(2)
where k goes from 1 to number of apneas, and n goes from 1 to
number of arousals. An apnea induced arousal is classified if
the arousal startpoint was placed between 5 seconds before and
10 seconds after the apnea startpoint. This interval is chosen,
since an arousal awakes the subject, and hence stops the apnea
event.
The apnea induced arousal classification is done for
obstructive apneas, central apneas, hypopneas, and for all ap-
neas (the three types combined). How the features (amplitude,
power, duration, and frequency) for apnea induced arousals are
calculated, is described next.
Arousal Related Features
For the MrOS and the CFS study, WASO, SL (only MrOS), and SE
are part of the available parameters for the datasets, and these
values are extracted and used in this study. It has not been pos-
sible to calculate the latter two features, since no lights off/on
are noted, and hence these features have not been calculated
for the ERCP dataset.
Based on the available hypnogram, the TST can be calcu-
lated as:
TST = length(Hypnogram) – length(Hypnogram == Wake)
2/60
(3)
where length(Hypnogram) is the number of sleep stage epochs,
and length(Hypnogram == Wake) is the number of epochs clas-
sified as wake. The subtraction is divided by two, and after-
wards divided by 60, in order to gain the TST in hours. The
TST is calculated based on the number of epochs classified as a
sleep stage. Based on the TST, the arousal index can be calcu-
lated as:
ArousalIndex = length(ArousalSections)
TST
(4)
where length(ArousalSection) is the number of arousals.
For each arousal the arousal amplitude is calculated as:
amplitude(n) = max(ArousalSection(n)) – min(ArousalSection(n))
(5)
where n goes from 1 to the number of arousals.
The arousal power for all arousals is calculated as:
power(n) = sum(ArousalSection(n)2)
length(ArousalSection(n)
(6)
The power is calculated as the sum of absolute squares, divided
by the signal length.
Each arousal duration is calculated as:
duration(n) = length(ArousalSection(n))
fs
(7)
where fs is the sampling frequency.
The frequency for each arousal is calculated as:
peaksmax = findpeaks(ArousalSection(n))
peaksmin = findpeaks(–ArousalSection(n))
frequency(n) = length(peaksmax) + length(peaksmin)
2 · duration
(8)
where findpeaks is an inbuilt MATLAB function, that finds all
peaks in a signal.
The TBA is calculated as:
TBA(n) = startpoint(n + 1) – endpoint(n)
fs
(9)
where "endpoint(n)" is the endpoint for an arousal, and "start-
point(n+1)" is the startpoint for the following arousal. Between
two arousals, a period of wake can occur, in order to exclude
this awakening, the TBA is also calculated as:
WBA(n) = length(find(Wake < startpoint(n + 1)
&Wake > endpoint(n)))
TBAwithoutwake = startpoint(n + 1) – endpoint(n) – WBA
fs
(10)
where WBA is wake between arousals, calculated as the number
of epochs classified as wake between two arousals.
The number of arousals are calculated as:
ArousalNo = length(ArousalSection)
(11)
where length(ArousalSection) indicates the number of arousal
detected by the arousal detector.
Sleep Stages Related Features
Based on the hypnogram, it is possible to find the number of
shifts between each sleep stage, this has been calculated as e.g.:
if (Hypnogram(h : h + 1) == [WakeN1])
then NoShiftsWN1 = NoShiftsWN1 + 1
(12)
where h goes from 1 to the number of epochs - 1. This is done
for all possible sleep stage transitions. The duration of each
sleep stage is calculated as:
SSduration = length(SS)
fs/60/60
(13)
where length(SS) is the number of epochs classified as the spe-
cific sleep stage. The number is divided by the sampling fre-
quency, and twice divided by 60, in order to obtain the duration
in hours. This is done for all sleep stages.
Each arousal is separated into which sleep stage they started
in, by:
if startpoint(n) == SS then SSidx(idx) = n
(14)
where SS is the specific sleep stage, and idx is the number of
arousals separated into the specific sleep stage.
==PAGE:120==
Rebecca B. Rusborg et al.
|
5
The arousal index for each sleep stage is calculated as:
ArousalIndexSS = length(ArousalSection)/SSduration
(15)
where length(ArousalSectionSS) is the number of arousal, be-
longing to the specific sleep stage.
Besides these, the arousal amplitude, power, duration, fre-
quency, TBA, TBA without wake, arousal number, and arousal
index are also calculated for each arousal in the specific sleep
stage.
Test for Significance
To test for significant group differences for the different
arousal features, a two-sample t-test is performed, using the
inbuilt MATLAB function ttest2.
The two-sample t-test is a
parametric test, which compares two independent data sam-
ples, with the test statistics:
t =
¯x – ¯y
r
s2xn +
s2y
m
(16)
where ¯x and ¯y are the sample means, s2
x and s2
y are the sample
std, and n and m are the sample sizes. The returned t value
is a test decision for the null hypothesis; that the two data
samples are from independent, random samples with normal
distribution, equal means, and equal, unknown variances. The
rejection or acceptance of the null hypothesis is based on a 5%
significance level [28].
The decision of a parametric test is made, since a normal
distribution is assumed in both the insomnia and control group,
and since a parametric test has more statistical power [29]. A
two-sample test is chosen, since it tests whether a sample is
both higher or lower than the other sample [30]. This is an
important feature, since it is unknown whether the insomnia
group, will have a higher or lower feature value, compared to
the control group.
Results
Arousal Specific Features
Some features are calculated for each arousal, and hence one
subject has multiple values, where other features result in
one value per subject. The features with only one value are;
arousal index, TST, WASO, sleep efficiency, sleep latency, num-
ber of sleep stage shifts, and number of arousals. The features
that has one value for each arousal are; amplitude, duration,
frequency, power, and TBA with and without including wake
epochs. For these features, an average, variance, and std value
is calculated to make the significance testing possible.
From Table 1, it can be seen that subjects with and with-
out insomnia, follow the same distribution and tendency. Only
three significant group differences are found; TST, WASO, and
sleep efficiency, but only in the MrOS dataset. No significant
group differences are found for the CFS or ERCP dataset.
The features have been tested in multidimensional spaces,
where two features are compared with each other. The scatter
plots for TBA vs number of arousals, TST vs arousal amplitude,
and TST vs number of arousals can be seen in Figure 1, the re-
maining scatter plots follows the same tendency. Even though
the scatter plots looks different, no clear group differences can
be seen. The insomnia group and control group cannot be sep-
arated, when using higher feature dimensions.

index for apnea induced arousals. The arousal index is calculated
as #arousalsApneaInduced/TST, where TST is the total sleep time, and

deemed significant at a 5% significant level.
The feature difference for apnea induced arousals compared
to non-apnea induced arousals, can be seen in Table 5 and
6.
The former shows in which sleep stages a significant
difference between apnea induced and non-apnea induced
occurs for subjects with insomnia, and the latter shows for
controls. These tables show that apnea has a large effect on
the arousal appearance, through all sleep stages. Most often,
the apneas decrease the arousal features, making the arousals
less prominent. Only the arousal duration, when looking at
the entire sleep, is increased by apnea induced arousal, or
more specifically hypopneas.
If comparing apnea and non-apnea induced arousal fea-
tures between subjects with insomnia and controls, it would
be expected to show a significant group difference.

shows where a significant group difference is found for the
arousal amplitude. The table shows that the group difference
is significant for almost all apnea and non-apnea induced
arousals in all sleep stages. Only the ERCP dataset experience
situations with no significant group differences; often when
