Table 8 shows that the CFS and MrOS have significant group
differences for the arousal power, for all apnea and non-apnea
induced arousals in all sleep stages, except obstructive apnea
induced arousal in wake, and non-apnea induced arousals in
N2 sleep, respectively. The ERCP dataset only has a significant
group difference in N1 and N2 sleep for all arousals, except
obstructive apnea induced arousals, in N3 sleep for obstructive
apnea induced arousals, and in the entire sleep for central
apnea induced arousals.
Table 9 shows
the
group differences for the
arousal
duration, and significant group differences for all apnea and
non-apnea induced arousals in all sleep stages. For the ERCP
dataset, the obstructive and central apnea induced arousals in
N1 and N3 sleep, respectively, does not result in a significant
group difference.
The arousal frequency has a significant
group difference for all apnea and non-apnea induced arousals,
except for obstructive apnea induced arousals in N1 sleep,
central apneas and hypopneas induced arousals in N3 sleep in
the ERCP dataset, see Table 10.
Sleep Stage Transitions Features
The sleep stage transitions have been investigated for each
dataset, and illustrated in a Markovian state diagram, see
Figure 2. Figure 2a shows that a significant group difference
for MrOS, is found for sleep stage transitions N2 →W, N3→W,
and N2 →REM, where the latter one has a higher probability
for controls. These significant group differences are not found
in the other datasets. In the CFS dataset, a significant group
difference is found for wake →REM, and from N3 →N3 sleep
stage transitions, both with a higher probability for controls,
see Figure 2b. For the ERCP dataset, only one significant group
difference is found; N3 →N2, which has a higher probability
for subjects with insomnia, see Figure 2c.
In Figure 2, some transitions are not illustrated, due to less
than half of the subjects in both groups experiencing these
transitions. These transitions are wake →N3, N1 →N3, N3 →
REM, REM →N3, and N3 →N1 for all datasets. The MrOS and
CFS also have less than half of the subjects experience N2 →N1,
and REM →N2, the latter one is only for the MrOS dataset. This
indicates a higher agreement, between which sleep stage tran-
sitions that do not occur, compared to those that occur more or
less in subjects with insomnia.
Discussion
The Analysed Datasets
The focus of the MrOS study was the relationship between
sleep disorders and vascular disease, falls, fractures, and
mortality in older men, and the focus of the CFS study was
sleep apnea. Only the ERCP study had a focus on insomnia,
but more specifically on the insomnia treatment.
Since the
focus of the studies were different than the focus of this
study, the results could be affected. However, when choosing
participants for the different studies, the subjects are chosen
based on the inclusion criteria that fit each study.
These
criteria do not focus on the insomnia disease, and hence the
subjects included might not be the typical insomnia patients.
If the subjects do not fit the typical insomnia group, then
the results might not be useful and applicable for research
regarding the insomnia disease.
However, all studies have
insomnia
related
information,
so
only
participants
with
insomnia related information is included in this study, which
should make the results applicable. The criteria of insomnia
related parameters, excluded some participants in the MrOS
and the CFS dataset. By making sure the subjects have an ISI
higher than 9, or have been diagnosed with insomnia, the
subjects should be a part of the typical insomnia disease group,
since they are expected to fulfil the criteria of the insomnia
definition. Hence, the results of this study, are not suspected
to be influenced or affected by the chosen studies population.
==PAGE:123==
8
|
SLEEPJ, 2020, Vol. 00, No. 0
Included Participants
From the MrOS study, 1,020 participants are included, from the
CFS study, 730 participants are included, and from the ERCP
study, 205 participants are included in this study, giving a total
of 1,955 participants. This is, from a technical view, believed to
be enough to express the diversity between the insomnia and
the control group.
Given that insomnia has multiple subtypes, it will not be
possible to comment on the meaning of the findings in all in-
somnia subtypes. However, the ERCP only consists of subjects
with PI, and the results can be used to comment on the findings
relation to PI. PI is often seen in younger women and rarely
seen in elderly, hence the MrOS is not expected to have any
subjects with PI included. Often, the insomnia seen in elderly
is due to other external factors, such as medicine and other dis-
eases. The insomnia group in the MrOS, is therefore expected
to be insomnia due to comorbidities. The CFS has subjects in
all ages, so determining which insomnia type the subjects have,
is not possible. It is most likely a combination of PI, insomnia
due to external factors and comorbidities, and other insomnia
subtypes.
Group Division
A group division based on insomnia related parameters is made.
For the MrOS and ERCP datasets, a group division is made based
on the ISI value, and for the CFS it is made based on a clinical
diagnosis of insomnia. The group division of the CFS study is
expected to be fairly representative of the insomnia and control
populations. The group division of the MrOS and ERCP studies
are only based on the ISI values, which is, independently, not
enough for a diagnosis. However, the ISI scores give an insight
into the sleep difficulty, and often an ISI value of 9 indicates
mild insomnia with increasing severity. When looking at Ta-
ble ??, a significant group difference is seen for all insomnia
related parameters, and the group division based on the ISI
values, is therefore believed to result in a good representation
of the groups.
Feature Comparison with the Literature
Other researchers have studied conventional sleep parameters,
when investigating group differences.
These studies do not
agree on which parameters that are significantly different,
between subjects with insomnia and controls.
Most studies
agree on a difference in TST, WASO, and SE. This is supported
by the findings in the MrOS dataset, but not in the CFS or ERCP
datasets, see Table 1. When looking at the different study pop-
ulations in the MrOS and CFS, the findings could suggest, that
the conventional sleep parameters are affected by insomnia
subtypes related to medicine or comorbidities. Thus, that the
sleep architecture is not affected by the insomnia disease, but
by other disorders and medication. This is also supported by
the findings in the ERCP, since no group difference is found
for the available conventional sleep parameters.
Table 1 also shows the arousal specific feature during the
entire sleep. These features do not result in any significant
group differences, indicating that the insomnia disease does
not affect the overall arousal appearance. The same applies for
the number of sleep stage transitions, the number of arousals,
and the TBA. The number of arousals was expected to result
in a significant group difference, since the subjects with
insomnia experience getting less sleep during the night. This
can not be supported by the findings, which could suggest
that patients with insomnia are more attentive to their sleep,
which could give the impression of being awake.
The number of arousals, sleep stage durations, and arousal
features for specific sleep stages,
did not result in any
significant group differences for all three datasets, see Table
2.
No similar significant group difference is found between
any two datasets. This could support the suggestion, that the
insomnia disease does not affect the number of arousals, their
appearance, or the time spent in each sleep stage.
Sleep Stage Transitions Differences
Based on the article by Yishul Wei et. al. [10], it is expected
to find an increased transitions probability for subjects with
insomnia between sleep stage N2 to stage N1 and to wake.
This is not found throughout this study, with the exception
of the transitions from stage N2 to wake in the MrOS dataset.
From the Markovian state diagrams in Figure 2a-2c,
no
significant group difference is found in more than one dataset.
When looking at the MrOS dataset, a significantly increased
transition probability for subjects with insomnia is found from
stage N2 to wake, and from N3 to wake. This could indicate,
that the subjects with insomnia are more likely to wake up
during sleep. A significantly increased transition probability
for controls is found from stage N2 to REM sleep, which could

Figure 2. Markovian state diagram. Red arrows indicate significantly higher
transition probability for subjects with insomnia, and blue arrows indicate sig-
nificantly lower probability. Gray arrows indicates no significant group differ-
ence. Transitions are not visualized if they did not occur in at least half of the
participants in each group.
indicate that the controls are more likely to go into deeper
sleep faster than subjects with insomnia.
For the CFS study, no significantly increased probabilities
for subjects with insomnia are found, which could indicate,
that the before mentioned difference, is due to comorbidities,
instead of the insomnia disease.
However, a significantly
increased transition from wake to REM sleep is found for
controls, which is the hallmark of narcolepsy [31]. Why this is
seen in controls is unclear.
When looking at the ERCP study, only one significant
difference is found; an increased probability for subjects with
insomnia to shift from stage N3 to N2. This could support the
findings in the MrOS dataset; that subjects with insomnia are
less likely to experience sustained deep sleep in comparison to
controls.
Apnea Induced Arousal Feature Differences
The apnea induced arousals have been defined based on the
arousal and apnea startpoints. However, since it is expected
that the apnea stops the arousals, it might provide better re-
sults if looking at the arousal endpoint and apnea startpoint.
However, it is expected to give the approximately same results,
and the findings are therefore still expected to be valid.
The number or arousal index of apnea induced arousals,
whether it is central apnea, obstructive apnea, hypopneas, or
all types of apneas, does not result in a significant group dif-
ference in any of the investigated datasets.
This indicates
that subjects with insomnia and controls experience the same
amount of apnea induced arousals.
However, when looking into the appearance of the apnea
and non-apnea induced arousals, a significant group differ-
ence is found for all arousal features and in all sleep stages
for the MrOS and CFS datasets. But, when looking at the ERCP,
a significant group difference is present most of the time for
the arousal duration and frequency. For the arousal amplitude,
the significant difference is present for most arousals in stage
N1, N2, REM, and through the entire sleep, and only for a few
arousal types in wake and N3 sleep. The arousal power is in
two thirds of the cases not significantly different between the
groups. This could indicate that the height of an apnea induced
arousal in subjects with PI is less affected, compared to the
arousal frequency and duration.
These findings could suggest, that non-apnea and apnea in-
duced arousals are highly affected by the insomnia disease, es-
pecially the frequency and duration, where the amplitude and
power is more affected by the reasons for the insomnia disease,
such as medicine or other diseases.
Reflection on the Arousal Definition
For arousal scoring in NREM sleep, only EEG channels are
needed, but when scored in REM sleep, EMG channels are
needed as well. Why should the motor activity only be con-
sidered in REM sleep, and not in NREM sleep? In NREM sleep,
an arousal event can be detected with ease, due to the NREM
activity appearance, but in REM sleep, arousals are hard to de-
tect, due to higher frequencies resembling wakefulness.
The definition of arousals is defined in the AASM man-
ual, which was established in 1975 [32], and the manual was
based on existing knowledge, hence the knowledge regarding
arousals are discovered before. The knowledge and computa-
tional resources have improved, which drives the needs for a
more consistent and updated arousal definition. The criteria,
regarding that the arousals need to proceed 10 seconds of stable
sleep, and that motor activity should be included in detection
during REM sleep, results in arousals being detected in NREM
==PAGE:127==
12
|
SLEEPJ, 2020, Vol. 00, No. 0
sleep, not to be considered as arousals in REM sleep. Today,
an arousal detection can be performed more consistently if us-
ing an automatic detector. The detector can detect very small
changes in the frequencies, and if the detector uses its own
definition of frequency shifts in the EEG, and not mimics man-
ual annotations, the needs for including EMG channels are not
necessary.
If the definition of arousals are outdated, this will influence
the hyperarousal theory regarding insomnia. This will be dis-
cussed in the next section.
Reflection on the Insomnia Definition
The definition of insomnia can be made, when one or more of
the following problems are present:
• Hard time initiating sleep
• Struggle to maintain sleep, and waking up frequently dur-
ing the night
• Wake up too early and being unable to go back to sleep
• The sleep is nonrestorative, or of poor quality
This definition is highly heterogenic, meaning that there could
be multiple causes explaining each problem. A hard time to
initiate sleep, could be due to e.g. too much light, loud noises,
worrying, pain, and waking up too early could be due to loud
noises, the bed partner, and/or the temperature etc. Due to
the many reasons for each problem, the definition is very
imprecise, making it hard to find specific elements during
the sleep, that are significantly different for subjects with
insomnia.
The hyperarousal theory, concerning insomnia, suggests
an increased arousability, though, this can not be verified
in this study, despite a large amount of subjects included.
The findings of this study, suggest that arousals and sleep
stage transitions do not cause insomnia. For subjects with PI,
the ERCP dataset, no clear, significant differences are found,
which questions the hypothesis. This study finds large group
differences, when looking at apnea induced arousals, which
indicates that what drives the occurrence of arousals, is other
factors such as sleep apnea. No clear group difference is found
for sleep stage transitions, and no markers that drives the
hypothesis underlying insomnia, are found.
Limitations
When performing e.g. 100 separate tests with a significant level
of 0.05, it is expected to result in five tests deemed signifi-
cant by chance, called type I error. The family-wise error rate
(FWER) describes the probability of at least one type I error,
and can be calculated as:
FWER = 1 – (1 – α)m
(17)
where FWER is the family-wise error rate, alpha is the sig-
nificant level, and m is the number of tests performed [33].
Throughout this study, approximately 400 significant tests
have been performed for each dataset, resulting in a FWER >
0.99.
This indicates that there is an ∼100% chance, that at
least one type I error has occured. This indicates, that using a
two-sample t-test, might not be an optimal choice, and should
be replaced by another, more accurate, test.
The FWER can be controlled using several methods, e.g. the
Bonferroni method. This method ensures the overall signifi-
cant level, by decreasing the significant level for each test to
α/m [34]. In the case of this study, the significant level for
each test would then be αbonferroni = 0.05/400 = 1.25 · 10–4. Us-
ing this significant level, fewer significant group differences
would be observed, e.g.
no significant group difference for
TST, WASO, and no arousal specific features in any sleep stages
would be found.
Conclusion
The purpose of this study, was to investigate specific physio-
logical features for insomnia, in order to gain more knowledge
and insight into the disease. To ensure that the study provided
new information to the research area, it was initiated with a
comprehensive literature study regarding state of the art work.
This was used in the decision regarding which features that
should be investigated.
The conventional sleep parameters were checked for signif-
icant group differences between subjects with insomnia and
controls, but no clear pattern was seen for subjects with insom-
nia. The same applies to features regarding the sleep stages
and their transitions.
However, significant differences were
found for the arousal appearance, when investigating arousals
induced by different apnea types. These findings suggest, that
patients with insomnia do not have increased arousability, and
the hyperarousal theory might not be correct or precise enough
for the current insomnia definition. The findings suggest, that
insomnia is driven by other factors, such as apnea.
This study therefore challenges the definition of the hyper-
arousal theory, since no clear correlation between subjects with
insomnia and arousal specific features, sleep stage transitions,
and conventional sleep parameters was found.

Main Bibliography
B. SEARCH STRATEGY
B
Search Strategy
A structured literature search was highly important, since it provided the necessary in-
formation regarding the research area, gave inspiration for new research areas, and pro-
vided a comparison basis.
The included studies were chosen, based on their relevance for the project goal of this
thesis, their methodology, and their results. Studies with focus on the insomnia dis-
ease, and how insomniacs differ from controls, were considered, as well as studies that
had developed an arousal detector. The search techniques are described below. A few
articles were provided by the supervisors, and did not follow the techniques below.
B.1
Screening Techniques
A speciﬁc free word search had been used to limit the number of search results to less
than 100. The titles of the search results were ﬁrst read through, in order to exclude
nondescript articles. Afterwards, the abstract of the remaining articles was read, to
further exclude non-relevant articles. Finally, the full articles were read of the remaining
studies, and only those still within the project scope were included.
B.2
Search engine
The scope of this thesis covered both medical aspects as well as technological. Due
to these two aspects, the library service of DTU, DTU Findit, and PubMed was used.
DTU Findit searched through multiple databases and journals, and covered 190 million
articles. PubMed searched through biomedical literature from MEDLINE, life science,
and online books, and covered more than 30 million articles. Both search engines were
used in this project.
B.3
Keywords
Multiple searches were performed, using free word search with different keywords. The
search was narrowed by searching for multiple words at a time. The words used, in
different combinations, were: insomnia, hyperarousal, data-driven, detection, arousal
characteristics, arousal, morphology, arousability, characteristics, analysis, EEG, sleep
architecture, features, attribute, detector, automatic, sleep, algorithm, and signal pro-
cessing.
99
==PAGE:130==
B. SEARCH STRATEGY
Main Bibliography
B.4
Inclusion Criteria
All searches were narrowed down, based on some inclusion criteria; 1) the articles were
published within the last 5 years, in order to ensure that the study was within state of
the art, and 2) the article needed to be available online.

D. GLOSSARY
D
Glossary
D.1
Finite & Inﬁnite Impulse Response
Both the ﬁnite impulse response (FIR) and the inﬁnite impulse response (IIR) are digital
ﬁlters. The equation for an FIR ﬁlter is:
y(n) =
N−1
X
k=0
akx(n −k)
(9.1)
and the equation for an IIR ﬁlter is:
y(n) =
q
X
k=0
akx(n −k) +
p
X
k=1
bky(n −k)
(9.2)
where x(n) is the ﬁlter input, and y(n) is the output. The mathematical difference is that
the IIR ﬁlter uses some previous outputs as input, and is thus a recursive function [20].
IIR ﬁlters are used when a linear phase is not important, and memory is limited. They
have a low implementation cost, meaning a lower order, coefﬁcients, and memory is
need to obtain the same cut-off frequencies as for FIR ﬁlters. They are suitable for real-
time control, due to its high speed. But, IIR ﬁlters have non-linear phase characteristics
near the cut-off frequencies, and are less numerically stable, due to the recursive func-
tion. If the IIR ﬁlter is combined with the zero-phase ﬁltering, hence the ﬁlter is applied
both forwards and backwards, the phase distortion is similar, and thus equalized. How-
ever, this cannot be done in real-time [20].
FIR ﬁlters are used when a linear phase is important, and a high amount of memory and
computation performance is available. The linear phase is an advantage, since no phase
distortion is present, and all frequencies are shifted similarly. They do not use previous
output values, and the ﬁlter is thus stable, independently of the given input. But, FIR
ﬁlters requires high computational performance and memory, since they need to use
more coefﬁcients, to achieve the same cut-off frequencies as for IIR ﬁlters. Due to the
high computational performances, FIR ﬁlters are less suitable for real-time control [20].
Polyphase ﬁlter
Any FIR and some IIR ﬁlters can be applied in a polyphase format. The polyphase
ﬁltering is a computationally efﬁcient way of applying resampling and ﬁltering to a
signal [21]. Normally, the ﬁltering will be done ﬁrst, followed by the downsampling,
in order to avoid aliasing. This process is done, by ﬁrst convolving a ﬁlter response
with the full signal, and then discard some or most of the calculated points. This is an
121
==PAGE:152==
D. GLOSSARY
Main Bibliography
inefﬁcient method, due to unnecessary calculations. Polyphase ﬁltering can, without
aliasing, downsample the signal before ﬁltering [21].
D.2
Kaiser Window
A window function is a mathematical function that is zero outside a chosen interval. The
Kaiser window is a simple approximation of the discrete prolate spheroidal sequence
(DPSS) window [22], which maximizes the energy in the main lobe, and reduces infor-
mation loss at the window edge [23].
D.3
MATLAB Function ﬁndpeaks
The MATLAB function ﬁndpeaks, returns a vector with the local maximum values, and
location of the input vector. The local peak is deﬁned as a data point, that is larger than
its two neighboring points, or is equal to inﬁnity [24].
D.4
Neural Networks
A neural network (NN) is used to recognize relationships in a dataset, by using a se-
ries of algorithms. These algorithms are performed by units, called neurons, which are
weighted connected with other neurons. The neurons are organized in layers, and con-
sists of a minimum of 3 layers; an input layer, a minimum of one hidden layer, and an
output layer. The input pattern is ﬁrst presented to the input layer, which is given an
activation, which then propagates to the hidden layer(s) and the output layer [25].
Convolutional Neural Networks
If the NN should classify an image of size 999x999, and the networks only contains one
hidden layer with 1000 neurons, then the ﬁrst layer would contain 999·999·1000 ≈109
weights. This is a highly computational burden, and there would doubtfully be enough
images to tune this amount of parameters [25].
Instead of a full connection, the computation burden can be reduced by only looking
at local regions. This means, that the neurons in the hidden layer, only receive inputs
from the corresponding region. If each neuron only contains information from an area
of 5x5, then the ﬁrst layer would have 5 · 5 · 1000 = 25, 000, weights, which is a drastic
improvement. To further reduce this number, the local connection weights can be kept
ﬁxed for the entire neurons, meaning that the neighbor neurons in the next layer, are
connected with the same weights. The weights then drops to 5 · 5 = 25. This last
simpliﬁcation is similar to sliding a window of 5x5 and mapping the output, which
allows for feature detection regardless of their position [26].
122
==PAGE:153==
Main Bibliography
D. GLOSSARY
Long Short-Term Memory Network
The long short-term memory (LSTM) network is a special kind of recurrent neural net-
works (RNN), which uses previous information to the present task. It is difﬁcult for the
RNN to carry information from earlier steps to later ones, if the sequence is long, and
hence might leave out important information. The LSTM is capable of learning long-
term dependencies, and due to its construction, it can remember the information for a
longer time [27].
Multi-Layer Perceptron
A multi-layer perceptron (MLP) consists of at least one hidden layer. Each layer con-
tains interconnected nodes, called perceptrons, which are mathematical functions, sim-
ilar to linear regression. The perceptrons feeds the signal to a non-linear activation
function, and hence can separate data that cannot be separated linearly [28].
D.5
Recursive Least Square Adaptive Filter
The recursive least square (RLS) is an adaptive ﬁlter algorithm, which ﬁnds the coefﬁ-
cients that will minimize the weighted linear least squares cost function. The RLS al-
gorithm has a fast convergence, due to a complicated mathematical operation and since
it requires more computational power. Due to the fast convergence, the RLS algorithm
has been widely used in real-time systems [29].
The signal d(n), with noise, is received as:
x(n) =
q
X
k=0
bn(k)d(n −k) + v(n)
where v(n) represents the noise. The intention is to recover the desired signal d(n), by
using a p+1-tap FIR ﬁlter, w:
d(n) ≈
p
X
k=0
w(k)x(n −k) = wTxn
where xn is a vector, containing the p+1 most recent samples. The estimate of the
desired signal is:
ˆd(n) =
p
X
k=0
wn(k)x(n −k) = wT
nxn
where wn is a vector containing the current estimate. ˆd(n) is a scalar. The estimation is
good, if ˆd(n) −d(n) is small [29].
